I am aware that I am not strictly following- the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the tieatm nt of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph , Nov.
6th, 7th and Bth. 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight those pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from these disease?, who are scattered over all parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider, therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which note exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
Iffy Qualification* I InviteAhe fullett Investigation Into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon to tKe
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observln { the many phases of the Nervous and
Sexua 1 System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is informed at tho
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
if I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, ivith whom we do not wish

SATURDAY, JANUARY 24, 1891.
Compulsory Voting.
The conviction is becoming widespread that,
so far, Representative Government, like the
mountebank at a rustic fair, has not fulfilled
the programme of its promise. In many
countries it has superseded the direct rule of
monarchs and of aristocracies ; but, in the
majority of cases, it would be hard to say
what real advantage the great body of the
people has derived from the transfer of
power. To the donkey in the fable it made
little difference whether he was burdened
and beaten by his master, the husbandman,
or by the invaders who were pouring into
the country, and who would, no doubt,
requisition him to assist in dragging an
ammunition-waggon. Yet the theory of
Representative Government is perfect. When
states or communities are small, the whole
people can assemble and make their laws
there and then without the intervention of
any delegates. When they are populous, or
the population scattered, such a process
would be impossible. It, therefore, becomes
necessary to choose certain men, instruct
them what to do, and let them do it. This
is simplicity itself. How comes it, then,
that in practice this attractively plain theory
so frequently fails'? We shall attempt to
point out some of the causes, and indicate at
least one remedy. Compulsory voting is the
latter. There is no reason to suppose that
it will solder all the holes in the State kettle—
but there is every reason to suppose that it
will stop some of them.
* # #
To explain matters it is necessary to go
back somewhat in time. The principle of
government by representation was discovered
and introduced into England by a French-
man named De Montfort. Possibly for
this, among other things, his head and hands
were cut off by Edward the First—the
greatest of the Plantagenets. Why the
monarch should have gone to the trouble of
removing the ingenious Frenchman’s hands,
having first removed his head, is not very
clear. This occurred considerably over six
hundred years ago, and, so decorously in
England does freedom “ broaden down from
precedent to precedent,” that to-day there
are perhaps millions of Englishmen who do
not enjoy the franchise. Indeed, men now
living can call to mind when half the
members of the House of Commons were
mere nominees of the aristocracy ; when the
whole United Kingdom contained but a few
thousand voters ; when seats were put up to
auction like cattle in a sale-yard, and when
some electorates contained but one voter
who always put himself in Parliament, except
when paid to keep out of it. To call such a
system a representative one merely shows -
that, on occasions, the Briton has a fine sense
of humour. It would, therefore, be mani-
festly unfair to assert that, because the
British system was never more than a quali-
fied success, and generally a mischievous
failure, parliamentary institutions stand con-
demned. It never was a truly representative
system from its rise in the thirteenth cen-
tury ; it is not now, in the nineteenth.
* * *
But there are countries where the franchise
is now and has long been, universal. -In such
lands, has the emancipation of the masses
brought about the benefits anticipated ? Un-
fortunately, the answer must be negative to
be true. Only a fraction of those entitled to
vote take the trouble to attend the poll at
all, while even the clearly-expressed wishes
of those who attend are too often thwarted
or subjected to irritating delays by the very
persons appointed to give them effect. It is
strange that it should be so, but it is true
as strange. Thus the precise object for
which popular rule was instituted—viz., to
quickly ascertain what a majority of the
people desired, and to rapidly carry that
desire into execution—is warped at the very
outset, by the supineness of the people
themselves in the first place, and by the
supineness or trickery of their delegates in
the second. We use the word delegate
advisedly. Many members of legislative
chambers object to it, and say, with some
hauteur, “We are not delegates, we are
representatives, and, as such, insist on venti-
lating our own opinions.” This position is
clearly untenable. Men are not elected to
express their own views, or to legislate as
they think proper. They are chosen to
express the views of others, and to make
the laws those others desire. If intending
candidates for Parliament have fixed opinions
of their own on the utility or inutility of
certain proposals, they should seek elec-
torates where such opinions are in the
ascendant. But it is only one remove from
impudence for a man to accept a commis-
sion to forward certain views and then
to delay or decline to act, as those
views are not consonant with his
own. It is as if a property-owner gave his
agents instructions to sell a house for £lOO,
and next day received information to say it
was disposed of for £5O, as the agent was of
opinion it was worth no more.
* * #
There is here a difficulty at the very out-
set, to which it is but fair to give due
Aveight. The majority of the electors of a
country may demand law reform, or local
government, or any other measure of a
comprehensive nature. But it would be
too much to expect them to go into all the
particulars or minutiae of the desired change.
They may not have time, or they may not
have training. In such cases, the margin
of individual opinion conferred upon
the delegate is necessarily widened. It
is, then, his duty to assist in giving pro-
per shape to the general tenor of his con-
stituents’ views on intricate subjects. Still,
no such latitude is allowed him in cases
which admit of but a simple “yes” or
“ no.” For him to equivocate or delay, or
say “ no,” when the electors deputed him to
say “yes,” is absurdity in excelsis , So far
as we have gone, then, it would appear that
nowhere has universal suffrage been so suc-
cessful as anticipated. If kings have been
deposed or rendered powerless, Cabinets
have taken their places. These Cabinets
lead instead of obeying. If aristocracies
have disappeared or been curtailed of their
privileges, Parliamentary majorities have
succeeded, so that with a few trifling
changes the masses of the people are much
where they were. Under what is called
Responsible Government, in Australia, and
more especially in New South Wales,
during the last few years, acts as
arbitrary have been done as under any
despotic monarch. Cabinets, in theory, are
supposed to be mere machines constructed
for but a single purpose—to carry into effect
the wish of the voters. Similarly, in theory,
the ballot-box is supposed to be the State’s
centre of gravity. How woefully short of
their theoretic value both suppositions fall is
known to the veriest tyro in public affairs.
* # *
The supineness of the ruled, and the
pursuit of self-interest on the part of the
rulers are the main factors in the compara-
tive failure of representative institutions. It
is volubility, not virtue, that carries the day.
A three-year tenure of power also, is
too long for any Parliament, particu-
larly a paid one. Annual, or at most
biennial, congresses should be the rule.
While the principle of payment of
of members is demonstrably sound, there is
an undoubted temptation offered by trien-
nial Parliaments to impecunious men to
dodge dissolutions. If it is said that
shorter terms would mean more frequently-
recurring popular turm oil, the answer is, in
the first place, that with vote by ballot there
is but little scope for undue excitement at
all ; and, in the second, that political agita-
tion is far preferable to political stagnation.
It is the swamp, not the stream, that breeds
most corruption. It may now be asked
“ what influence will obligatory voting have
on this deplorable state of affairs—how will
it mend matters ?” As intimated a few
paragraphs back, we do not put it forward
as a specific, but as a useful palliative. To
find a true specific for these and many other
ills—social, sexual and political—it is neces-
sary to travel far—to proceed until the high
portals seen by some of the most gifted of
men’s children are passed, and the fair
domain of Socialism comes in view. Not, be
it remembered, the phantom Socialism of the
raw head-and-bloody-bones, or Phrygian cap
pattern—the only kind known to the smug
capitalist who loafs oir the labours of others
--but the system that gives to every child
born into the world an equal start in the
race of life.
# # #
Suppose a constituency of 100 electors, 50
of whom refrain from voting. The candi-
date, say, is returned by a majority of one ;
in other words, he is pledged to carry out
the views of one-fourth of his constituency.
He may take it upon himself to ignore that
pledge, and yet be returned by a much
larger majority at the next election. For
many of those who abstained from attending
the poll on the one occasion may come
forward on the other, and uphold him in his
recalcitration. But if all, or nearly all, had
voted in the first instance, the probability of
his ratting would be reduced to a minimum.
The representative who, with a record of
broken pledges or of aimless obstruction,
would face a half-voting electorate with a
light heart, would have many misgivings
when facing a constituency where every
vote was polled on his previous wooing, and
where he was aware that every vote would
be recorded on his subsequent appeal.
* # #
Even under our present social and political
system of individualism, where, in popular
language, the motto is “ everyone for him-
self and the Devil take the hindmost,” the
citizen is under obligations which he is not
permitted to ignore. He is obliged to
Answer the questions set before him in a
census-paper ; to serve on juries when called
upon ; to take a part in defending the
country from invasion ; to assist the police
in securing a prisoner or quelling a disturb-
ance ; to see that his children attend school
during certain periods ; and to perform
other duties having for their object the pre-
servation or progress of society. It is only
by risking his lucre or his liberty that he
can refuse to comply with these obligations.
But if it be penal to keep children from
school lest they should grow up in ignorance
and so endanger society’s safety, how much
more deserving of penalties is it to endanger
the well-being of that society by neglecting
to take a part in choosing the delegates who
are to lay down laws for its guidance ? In
many countries men are deprived of their
civil rights for various offences. It may at
first sight seem absurd to deprive men of
the right of voting when, while possessing
it, they evince no desire to exercise it. It
appears like depriving a man of a privilege
of which he would gladly be rid. Yet there
is little doubt that a law disfranchising for a
longer or shorter term those who so far for-
get their duties as to neglect recording their
votes, would have a salutary effect. If by
experience it was found that they thought
little of the deprivation, other means for
impressing upon them their duties would
speedily be devised. With society as at
present constituted we, in common with
many who have devoted time and attention
to the momentous social problems pressing
for solution, are by no means enamoured.
But while men who wish to leave the world
better than they found it look forward with
confidence to the advent of a society based
upon the best rather than upon the worst
human qualities—founded upon man’s nobler
instincts, instead of upon his vilest—they
see no reason why the most may not
be made of things as they are. In a
word, they see no reason why the person
who has the right to vote for a delegate
commissioned to make laws binding on all
should not be compelled to exercise that
right, just as he is compelled to give his
children a schooling which tends to enhance
their value as citizens of a presumedly free
community.
A“Vigorous Borrowing Policy."
One of the most conspicuously probable state-
ments which has been published in the Austra.
lian press for the last ten years is that which
announces the intention of the new Government
at Perth to pursue “ a vigorous borrowing
policy,” for the expansion of the country and
the greater profit of the land-grabber and the
monopolist generally. It is very seldom indeed
that Australia is not borrowing vigorously, and
as further examples of this great national
tendency New South Wales and Victoria have
simultaneously resolved to float another huge
1.0. U. apiece on the London market, and
Queensland has her impecunious eye fixed in
the same direction, and South Australia and
Tasmania are expected to follow the general
example at a later period in the year, and they
all intend simply to borrow the money and pass
the consequences on to posterity. As a rule
the Australian States are tolerably unanimou s
in the matter of loans, but this year they are a
little more unanimous than usual because there
is an unexpected superfluity of money in
England, and the English investor has even a
greater difficulty than usual in finding a profit-
able outlet for his surplus cash. Nine-tenths of
the English nation, it is true, have virtually no
cash at all ; and at least one-tenth consists of a
mass of practically homeless outcasts who are
starving amid the snow and frost; and the
hungry pauperism of England is sufficient to
make a procession of human skeletons three
hundred miles in length, but the statement
that money is abundant takes no notice of these
facts. It merely signifies that somewhere
about one million inhabitants of the United
Kingdom are a little better off than usual, and,
therefore, that the others are a little worse off
and more liable to freeze and die and go to
eternal perdition ; and in consequence of these
things the market is in a favourable conditiot
for further borrowing.
* # #
A period of conspicuous prosperity in any oi
the Australian states is invariably the period
which immediately follows the raising of a big
loan, and the next period of prosperity is when
another big loan has been raised to enable the
colony to stagger along under the burden of the
one which went before it. In between the two
loans there is always a time of depression, and
an unemployed problem, and a shortage in the
national finances, and the only known remedy
for any of these evils is some more loans. In
these intervals of artificial poverty there is
manifested a tendency on the part of the hun-
gry, unreasoning masses to vote against the
(Government on the general principle that some-
thing is wrong, and someone must be respon-
sible for the wrong, and there is also a growing
inclination to rise, in a vague, uncertain way,
against the stupendous monopolies of land and
capital, which have already so utterly ruined
Australia that even a whole continent cannot
provide employment and bread for four millions
of inhabitants. And when a more or less intel-
ligent nation begins to demand the reason why
it should be hungry in the midst of an empty
Paradise, and gropes in the dark with only an
unutterable sense of injury to guide it for the
cause of its troubles, it is necessary to do some-
thing in order to postpone the day of reckoning
and make the problem last long enough to be
handed down to posterity. The readiest way
to shove the great difficulty on to the ensuing
generation lies in that process which is termed
the “absorption of surplus labour,” and the
easiest means of absorbing surplus labour is the
construction of a new railway in some unoccu-
pied region of drought and old, extinct craters;
and, therefore, as already mentioned, the
Government floats another loan, and leaves the
repayment of the same to whomever may chance
to come after.
* 4 #
The immediate effects of tfris progress are
various and complex. The first result of the
new railways or other public works is to boom
the property of the adjacent capitalist, and this
process is erroneously termed a revival of pro-
gress. Generally that astute proprietor takes
advantage of the unnatural inflation to sell out
in small lots, and at an enormous profit, to a
race of simple-minded inhabitants ; and then he
probably starts for England with the proceeds,
and becomes an honored and respected citizen in
the land of his fathers. By this proceeding
the first half of the loan generally vanishes out of
Australia shortly before the second half is mopped
as interest by British financiers who made pre-
vious advances. When the borrowed money is
exhausted, and the sudden financial delirium has
diet! away, the property which has been thus
traded off on the community generally falls
back in value, and the speculators, who mistook
a brief attack of monetary drunkenness for an
evidence of permanent prosperity, are badly
left. Then the townships which were marked
out in the wilderness vanish again into their
primitive obscurity, and the dingo howls on the
alleged “esplanade,” and the wallaby hops
over the site where the the Town-hall ought to
have been, and ultimately the Financial Agency
or Universal Permanent Discount Association-
run by British capital—forecloses on the lot,
and pays a considerable dividend in London on
the strength of the transaction. But for the
time, at all events, the public distress is
alleviated and the alleged “surplus labour”
finds employment. A great part of the loan
goes in buying land at an enormous valuation
to clear the way for the new improvements,
and most of the balance is spent on imported
railway and other material; but still a small
amount is left to be expended in wages, in
order that the electors of the colony may not
be provoked into firing the Government into
the gutter. But, as a rule, the sum which is
actually expended in the country is barely
enough to pay the first year’s deficiency on the
new lines and the loss incurred through the
breaking of the land-boom which attended the
undertaking in its earlier stages, and the
interest of the loan itself is a dead-weight upon
the inhabitants; and consequently the old
scarcity and the old “unemployed difficulty”
start afresh almost before the alleged ‘ ‘ wave of
prosperity ” has fairly begun. Under these
circumstances the Government generally floats
another loan, because it lias never learned how
to do anything else, and passes the difficulty
on, just as it has been passed on ever since
borrowing first began.
* * *
Every now and then, however, Australia
undergoes a species of political awakening and
realises suddenly that some particular Govern-
ment is borrowing too freely and too fast, but,
as a rule, this fact only dawns on the public
comprehension when the loans have been raised
and spent, and the inevitable reaction has come
rounef. Then the Opposition appeals to the
people to put an end to this long period of
criminal extravagance, and the people, in the
majority of cases, respond nobly to the invita-
tion. In Australia Ministries almost invariably
fall on the question of finance, and when they
fall it is generally discovered that the loan
money has all been dissipated, and the trust-
funds borrowed, and that there is nothing in
particular remaining, except a shortage. Then
the new Ministry—which took office for the
express purpose of putting an end to borrowing
—is reluctantly compelled to raise a fresh loan
to finish the public works left uncompleted by
its predecessor, and to replace the trust funds,
and put things straight generally ; and when
these results are achieved it is generally driven
to the dire extremity of raising some more loans
to absorb, for the fiftieth time, that “surplus
labour ” which is floating about in a loose,
melancholy, unfriended state through tire
depression brought on by the fiendish Govern-
ment which went before it And when it has
finished doing these things, the previous
Government —now in Opposition usu illy starts
a fresh crusade because of the criminal stupidity
of the old Opposition—which is now the
Government; and when the two exchange
places once more the same old shortage turns
up just as it turned up previously, and there is
the same trouble about the borrowed trust-
funds and the unfinished public works, and the


“ surplus labour ” is found drifting about loose
in the same ancient style for want of some new
public works to give it employment. It is
needless to mention that, under these condi-
tions, the new Government starts by reluctantly
raising another loan. Even the sun is not more
regular than the inevitable loan.
# * #
The debts of Australia are an ever-increasing
quantity. In periods of prosperity the surplus
is invariably fooled away in increased salaries,
in bonuses to new industries or alleged indus-
tries, in prospecting-votes for the purpose of
hiring some politician’s uncle or relative to go
forth into the wilderness and look for gold
where no gold exists, in remissions of taxation
for the purpose of making the Cabinet’s calling
and re-election sure—in anything and every-
thing, in short, save in the reduction of the
nation’s liabilities; and in the periods of de-
pression which inevitably follow more liabilities
are floated to raise another burst of affluence.
The idea of ever wiping off any part of the
great national promissory-note is apparently
as foreign to the modern politician as the idea
of reverting to cannibalism and general naked-
ness ; he can no more grasp such a conception
than he can conceive the possibility of abolish-
ing feet, and running mankind on wheels. The
fact that a partial equivalent in the shape of
public w-orks will be handed down to futurity
along with this accumulation of debts and diffi-
culties is no justification, for the statesmen of
the present have traded away almost the entire
available surface of Australia, and this loss has
no approximate equivalent in the dubious legacy
of railways, irrigation works, and other assets
which will be passed on to the people of
succeeding years. The debts of this continent
represent actually nothing save the great prin-
ciple of living only for the present, and bequeath-
ing the insolvency and general smash which
may follow to the nation which comes after.
* * *
Australia, though poor, so far as the actual
amount of its wealth is concerned, is, in pro-
portion to population, almost the richest nation
to be found on earth. Yet even a whole conti-
nent cannot support 4,000,000 inhabitants when
the race of landlords, capitalists, importers,
miscellaneous monopolists and assorted finan-
ciers demand so much, and leave so very little
for the people at large. The “ surplus labour,”
which forms an ever-recurring problem in
Australian politics, represents that portion of
the nation which has been left behind in the
great scramble, because this continent is not
large enough to go round in the existing state
of society, and if the “surplus labourer” of
the present escapes from his unhappy condition,
and becomes a capitalist himself, lie can only
achieve that result by dragging someone else
down into the place which he lias quitted. If
Australia were ten times larger and more fertile
than it is, the landlord and the capitalist would
be richer, but the surplus labour would still
remain ; and if a thousand millions were
borrowed in place of ten, it would merely
postpone the difficulty for a few years, but the
remedy would remain as far off as ever. The
loan policy of Australia and the emigration
policy of “General” Booth in England are
two widely different expedients for the same
end ; the one seeks to shunt the question of
poverty on to a foreign country, and the other
to shunt the same question on to another
generation, but each is framed for the purpose
of passing the disease on to someone else, and
making things last a little while longer. But
when the Englishman can find no more places
to emigrate to, and the Australian can find no
one else to borrow from, then both nations will
have to face the question—Why the two richest
countries on earth cannot find employment and
food for their inhabitants? and somehow or
other the answer must be supplied.
The Bureaucrat and the People.
Dear Bulletin, — The gradual encroachment of
bureaucratic power on the liberties of the
people is the greatest danger which now
menaces national freedom. In no quarter of
the globe is this truth more apparent than in
Australia. The facts lately elicited in con-
nection with the maladministration of the New
South Wales Postal and Telegraph Departments
are a natural sequence to the vindictive sentence
Sassed upon Reseigh Martin for libelling the
r.S.W. Chief Commissioner for Railways.
# # #
It seems that the policy of the bureaucratic
ring is to cover with an impenetrable veil of
secresy the internal economy of every depart-
ment—to hide from the public those official acts
which concern the public most Can any cogent
reason be advanced in support of official secrecy
in the working of any department of the public
service, save that of the police ? Can any just
cause be alleged why arbitrary power should be
conferred on heads of departments ? By what
just right are civil servants paid princely
salaries from the public purse, their life interest
in permanent and lucrative employment secured,
and their frequent misfeasances either defended
or cloaked ? Have the public, then, no voice in
the administration of departments—have they
no voice in the government of the country, no
right to freely criticise the official action of a
well-paid public servant simply because he is
well-paid, and an overbearing Jack-in-office
“ clad in a little brief authority ?”
* # *
Freemen claim the right of free speech. The
servants of the public should be accountable
directly to the public ; they have, in common
with other members of a democratic community,
an opportunity to defend themselves from un-
just aspersion or unmerited calumny ; but it is
no more criminal, for any citizen to denounce,
through an erroneous report, the supposed
malfeasance of a bureaucrat than it is for an
audience in a theatre to hiss an unpopular-
actor. it,
* » tf *
The tactics of a certain section of the Govern-
ment in striving to suppress free speech, to
fetter and corrupt the Press, are all too obvious.
The proposal of a prominent politician to compel
contributors to the press to publish their “real
names ” in connection with their effusions, is
worthy of the spirit which vindictively en-
deavours to discover the author of the “ Post-
Office ” letters in the Protestant Stan-
dard. This course, if adopted, would
compel literary men who wished to benefit
or instruct the public, and who could not
conveniently seek publicity, to dictate their
“ copy ” to some ignorant jackdaw enabled thus
to strut in the borrowed plumage of real literary
merit. And everything adverse to political
rascality could be burked, unless the writer, in
publishing his name, could successfully resist
the onslaught of the whole pack of political
wolves and bureaucratic alligators, with their
legion of mercenary hangers-on and supporters.
* *
The utterances of the Maitland Mercury
and other New South Wales provincial papers
on the Eddy-Martin case indicate that very
few independent and outspoken papers exist in
New South Wales. The remainder are all
either poisonously subsidised by Government
advertisements, or by commercial advertise-
ments, or are supported by sectarian societies
subject to political cliques. When an estab-
lished newspaper becomes prosperous through
the sale of its principles, when it is wedded
solely to commercial interests, it becomes the
veriest slave of Mammon, the tool of the
oppressor, the advocate of legalised wrong, the
most powerful instrument of the political char-
latan and a curse to democracy. The “inde-
pendence ” of such papers is but the freedom of
a street sandwich-man. In the struggle of
National Freedom v. Bureaucracy it is all too
evident which side they will take.—Yours,
Australian.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Imported Titles.
Melbourne Age, anxious to rehabilitate its
former reputation for radicalism, has printed
an article which implies that the knighthoods
of MacBain, Davies, Benjamin and Co. are
contemptible jokes, and the baronetcy of
William Clarke a scandalous shame. The
chestnuts of the Age are very old, and have
appeared in The Bulletin any time during the
past ten years, but they are still sound and
good, and worth requoting. Says the Mel-
bourne journal of the local K.C.M.C.’s : —
If their titles perish with them no great harm will be
done ; they will spread their feathers peacock fashion,
exciting the envious admiration of a few simpletons, and
affording the many a laugh not quite untinged with con-
tempt. But to titles which descend from hither to son,
from the shrewd man who made the money to the com-
placent booby who squanders it, wc object much more
strongly, A snobocracy we must have, human nature
being what it is, but we need not predestinate men snobs
by making them heirs to titles which will cause a little
mob of sycophants to fawn on them from the hour at
which they cut their teeth to the day on which they lie
on their state-beds ready trussed for the undertaker.
From The Bulletin —in whose columns titles
are used as handles for turning ridicule upon
the system which creates them, or upon the
men who bear the badges — such plain truths
come with a good grace. But the Age stultifies
its burst of candour in every issue of the journal
by going out of its way to give these people the
satisfaction of seeing their “ Crown’’-granted
prefixes and affixes published in all seriousness.
We have ere now referred to the disgusting
alacrity with which newspapers make small
paragraph announcements of paltry events,
merely for the sake of working in the name of
some Sir Ponderous Ass, K.C.M.G,, or Sir
Addled Boozer, Bart., who puffed out a few re-
marks on the occasion in question. The Age is
every whit as bad as its dreary contemporaries
in this respect—ever ready to confer the dignity
of print, serious print, upon those titles that,
as it occasionally admits, never ought to have
been introduced into this country. And again,
we say that the daily Press has the power to
nullify the so-called “ honours ” conferred upon
our Australian bloatocracy by declining to let
the public know when the purchase of those
blessed honours is completed, and by reli-
giously refraining from any mention of any
Australian title whatsoever. Then, when a
person addressed Binjy Binjy as plain
“mister,” and Binjy replied, “Sir,” if you
please, the other person would merely say,
“ What are you giving us, Mister?” and pass
on. Whereupon Binjy would totter home and
die of baffled ambition, and when his obituary-
notice, setting forth his full title, was sent
round to the papers, the clerks would strike
out all the tommyrot and the world would
only notice that plain B.B. was no more.
———
In the Queen’s Name.
The Law, always ready to recognise distinction
of persons in favour of “ persons of distinction,”
makes no attempt to understand that some of
its statutes, although seeming to be absolutely
impartial in their dealings, are apt to press hard
or lightly upon citizens, just as they may chance
to be poor or rich. For example, Joshua Wood-
house has been fined (at the London County
Sessions), under a law which equally applies to
Australia, £20 for refusing to assist a police
constable when called upon by that functionary
in the Queen’s name. The constable was holding
on to an alleged thief, whom several roughs were
endeavouring to rescue, when he appealed to
Joshua. It was the duty of Woodhouse to
respond cheerfully, no doubt, but instead of
going straightway on the “ stoush” for the
glory of Victoria Regina, this unimpetuous
vulgarian put his hard, horny hands into the
pockets of his corduroys and walked away, say-
ing ; “ Not me ; I don’t want to get a hiding.”
Whereupon the law fined Joshua £20, as
it might have fined a “gentleman” for
a like failure to do his obvious duty
by the State — no man, Monarchist or
Republican, being legally entitled to plead
anything short of a bad leg as an excuse for
not coming up to the scratch under such cir-
cumstances. Yet Joshua, by rushing to the
assistance of the officer in distress, would
expose himself to risks (beyond the immediate
risk of being knocked silly) that don’t threaten
the casual influential stranger who does like-
wise. The latter, dwelling on the lofty eleva-
tion of respectability, can contrive to ignore
the existence of those very roughs who would
have constant opportunities for making things
awkward for Josh hereafter. The fear that he
might be privately requested to “wait until
they got him down their alley,” probably
decided Josh in favour of putting his hands in
his pockets. Maybe, too, he was mindful of
the social discomforts, not to say perils, which
might descend upon Mrs. Josh and the kids if
their “old man” got himself disliked by the
neighbours. Perhaps conjugal affection was at
the bottom of this poor, common person’s in-
disposition to offend men who could embitter the
whole future existence of his bride—but this
is a somewhat poetical conjecture. Asa matter
of hard fact the inhabitant of a “low” neigh-
bourhood stands, in the event of being called
upon to act as a special constable, at a serious
disadvantage compared with his wealthy fellow-
countrymen, and prudential ulterior considera-
tions, which need not trouble the citizen of
good means, may have naturally a deterrent
influence upon the struggling workman’s desire
to do the correct thing. All the ravings of high
morality and exalted patriotism cannot overrule
this practical truth. We don’t say that Joshua
Woodhouse, having little of his own to get
stolen, was less interested in the capture of a
thief than the Duke of Westminster would be,
but merely that he had a greater number of
possible consequences to fear. Yet, lacking wit
or education to put his case properly before a
Court that would rack its brains to find ex-
teimating circumstances for any wealthy misde-
meanant, Josh was fined by the Court just as
much as it could have fined a capitalist charged
with a less excusable shortcoming. Which was
another illustration of how the Law attempts
to atone for its flagrant injustice at some times
by being culpably stupid at others.

“ A Majority.”
The N.Z. Herald has struck a fresh mule—
this is a complicated figure of speech, but it ex-
presses the situation very well—on the advan-
tages of landlordism and the horrors of not
having a landlord :
Take the case of the 84,000 landowners of New Zealand.
Suppose them to be gradually replaced through legisla-
tive confiscation by a similar number of State tenants.
Reckoning population upon the basis of five to a family,
these holders under Government would represent 420,000
out of 020,000 souls, or two-thirds of the whole. This
majority would be a perpetual menace to the Govern-
ment, moulding it to its will. It could secure the almost
indefinite extension of the terms of tenancy. It would
have ample voting power to obtain the gradual lowering,
and, if so willed, the extinction of the tax. It requires
no great degree of foresight to convince us that
Georgianism worked out in fact must embark the country
adopting it in one perpetual round of disastrous revolu-
tions.
It would be a relief if the animal which in-
vented this argument would kindly come out of
his stable for five seconds and answer a few
questions. First of all, why should 420,000
tenants be more calculated to wreck the Maori
commonwealth than 420,000 landlords ? The
tenants’ majority, it seems, would be “a per-
petual menace to the Government, moulding it
at will,” but as the landlords are doing the
same just now things would be about square,
and anyhow the majority generally “moulds”
the Government—if the Herald found the
Government being moulded by anything else it
would probably complain about it. Secondly,
if the tenants would refuse to pay taxes because
they were the majority, why doesn’t the whole
nation refuse to pay now on the same basis, for
if it isn’t the majority in the largest sense of the
term, then the word is generally misunderstood?
Thirdly, if a majority is a “perpetual menace
to the Government,” what does the Herald
propose to do with it, and how will it fix up a
State in which everybody is in a minority and
every minority is smaller than every other
minority, so as to prevent the biggest minority
being a majority after all ? Fourthly, the fact
that the majority, under the Land Nationalisa-
tion system, could secure an “ almost indefinite
extension of the terms of tenancy ” might be an
evil, but as the same majority could equally
well confiscate the Herald, and hang the
editor, and put the Government in a bag with
a large millstone, and drop the whole concern
into the sea, we would be glad to know why
the affrighted organ of landlordism is so much
scared about a small danger and pays no atten-
tion to a very large one ? And lastly, it would
be interesting to learn if there ever was a sys-
tem under which either a majority, or a
minority, or anything else, couldn’t make an
ass of itself if it wanted to, either with or with-
out Land Nationalisation ; for if such a system
can be found The Bulletin is prepared to ad-
vocate it forthwith ? These questions suggest
five of the ways in which the Herald has made
a fool of itself, but there are about sixty more
which we are compelled to hold over for wane
of space.
An Inference by a Deranged Newspaper.
The Brisbane Week publishes the cold remains
of a murdered inference with regard to the
Scotch railway-strike, as follows :
The strike has hardly commenced before one of the
usual sequences of strikes presents itself—the need
of public subscriptions to carry on the work. This latter
fact points to the conclusion that a good many of the
men felt that there wag not a sufficient cause for a strike,
or that a strike was not a suitable remedy. In nearly all
these cases there is an attempt to compel men blindly
to follow their leaders—to reduce them to a state of
villeinage as complete as that of England 700 years ago.
When a crowd of men begin blacklegging
during a strike the capitalistic press always
advances the same old argument—that the
fact of blacklegs being obtainable is a suffi-
cient proof that there is no cause for dissatis-
faction on the part of Labour, and the
labourer’s lot is really all that any white man
can possibly desire. It is never, by any
chance, a proof that when men have to choose
between sixteen hours’ daily toil for 18s. or 20s.
a week, on the one hand, and utter starvation
in a frozen climate on the other, some of them
may accept the drudgery and the miserable
pittance as the smaller curse of the two—it is
rather a distinct evidence that the drudgery is
a pleasant and cheerful thing, and that the
average human being really likes it. Of course,
if the workman hangs himself as a third
alternative, that fact is not regarded as
proof that he is fond of hanging him-
self, and that he did it because he
would rather hang himself than remain un-
hanged, and that hanging is a pleasant thing
generally ; and if he goes out and brains an
employer it is no sufficient testimony that
employers ought to be brained, and that they
are really fond of the operation. When the
famished toiler accepts one method of escaping
from utter starvation he thereby demonstrates
that he has no grievances at all, and never had
any, and if he accepts either of the two other
equally rational methods he merely demon-
strates that he is a scoundrel and ought to go to
Sheol or thereabouts, but by no possible method
can he demonstrate that he is overworked and
underpaid, and the mere slave of an unfeeling
and ruffianly rich man or corporation. Also it
is a subject of complaint that he wants money
to help him in his difficulties, and though
Britain has put up countless millions for Hun-
garian rebels, and Greek insurgents, and dis-
affected Bulgarians, and inundated Chinamen,
and corpulent missionaries, it is an evidence of
utter depravity that a British workman should
want anything subscribed for him. And,
lastly, it is an unfathomable sin that he should
“blindly” follow the leaders he has himself
appointed, instead of blindly following the
employer who tells him to work for starvation
wages, or the aristocrat rulers who tell him he
must pay taxes for wars which were undertaken
a century before he was born, or the politicians
who bid him liquidate the bill for Egyptian
campaigns from which he derives no benefit.
Truly this world is full of logic—as well as of
other tinners which it is unnecessary to specify

Whatever Freemasonry may be in private
theory, it is the most lickspittling of institu-
tions in public fact. We read that the Earl of
Euston (a discredited person who nearly
died last week) opened anew Masonic
Hall at Northampton on 25th September.
After breathing his awful blessing on the
place, dear brother Grand Master Euston
opened a banquet, attended by a lot of local
Masons as well as visitors from all parts of the
world. What does Freemasonry inculcate
amongst its adherents, except a disposition to
exalt the wealthiest numbskull, or high-titled
blackguard, they can pitchfork into a position
for which, according to their own secret laws,
the said fool or “ duffer” hasn’t properly quali-
fied himself ? What is Australian Masonry, so
far as we see it in public, but an ostentatious
worship of Governors and people like Sir
William Clarke, which latter personage may
be specially instanced as mentally incapable of
“getting up” the dark Masonic lore that is
supposed to be inseparable from his high-
sounding billets ? If the careless reader—Free-
mason or otherwise —will think hard over these
matters for five minutes, he will understand
why Albert Edward, Prince of Wales, and
his male relatives and pals are always so ready
to open another blessed lodge, and w iro fraternal
greetings all over the earth.

We have read the evidence in the case of Mrs.
Pearcey, the now notorious Hampstead (Eng.)
murderess, who killed the wife and child of her
paramour, Hogg. When she heard of Hogg’s
proposed marriage, and became aware that he
could not get out of it, she wrote, despairingly :
—“I love you with all my heart, and I love
her because she will belong to you,” and much
more in a strain which showed her to be the
victim of a hopeless and overmastering infatu-
ation. To argue that the gallows (the woman
has since been hanged) acts as a deterrent in the
case of people who are swayed by the strong
passions which obviously ruled Mrs. Pearcey
(and which rule all real murderers) is simply to
convict oneself of sheer lunacy. The only
persons in whose minds the gallows is ever a
factor are they who are too weak and irresolute
or unimpressionable ever to commit murder.
What, for instance, will be the moral deducible
from the execution of Phelan (should he be
convicted) who, in a fit of jealousy killed his
wife at Melbourne, and then nearly succeeded
in killing himself ? Will it not be simply this
—“ When you, acting under an influence which
you cannot resist, commit murder, be sure and
have a knife sharp enough to kill yourself
afterwards.” Phelan wasn’t afraid either to
kill his victim or to kill himself—he did both to
the best of his ability. Now, if hanged, he
will be hanged simply because he by a mere
accident failed to absolutely commit suicide !
They might, as logically and as beneficially,
hang his knife-grinder.

The appeal case to the Privy Council, Ah Toy
v. Musgrove, evokes some facts about that
tribunal. It is smugly referred to as an evi-
dence of Imperial Federation, the last resort of
Australasia, Canada, India, Hong Kong, and
all the British Congeries. Yet it is not binding
on the House of Lords —only on us children,
not yet out of pap. A remarkable thing about
the Privy Council is that its business is con-
ducted without any fuss or feathers. No wigs
or gowns are worn, and the sittings are in a
comparatively small room at the Downing-street
office. The arguments go on quite in a conver-
sational way, there is no “orating,” and the
Bench is down on a level with the Bar, in fact,
there is literally no Bench at all. In short,
this is the only tribunal in the glorious empire
presided over by common-sense.
The Times, speaking of the prospects in the
Soudan, remarks in a gladsome way that the
Mahdi’s power has been completely broken,
and consequently “a large market for Man-
chester goods has been opened.” It was ever
thus. England went to the Soudan to
christianise the lost heathen and civilise him,
and having got there she merely trades off the
old slop shirt with the glaring stripes on the
tail, and if the Arab won’t bUy that garment he
is shot as being in a state of insurrection. The
talk about the spread of Christianity is too
thin, for no Moslem ever yet became a
Christian ; but if the market for Manchester
goods is opened nobodv cares whether the
Arab is converted or not. But merely for the
sake of appearances the British parson rises
with a stationary glass tear on the end of his
nose, and states that a great work is in progress,
and that the seed has been sown in the beloved
brown brudder who once languished in error’s
chain, but who now cannot be seen for dust as
he spurts along the road for Zion and crashes
heavily on the shingle of the New Jerusalem.

My Dear Mooraoixda,—
Lord Jersey landed at Princes’ Stairs, Sydney, ns per
advertisement, on 15th inst.* Of course all the available
great men met him on the brink of the harbour, but
owing to the fact that so many of the white-waistcoat
brigade are at present recruiting at their various country
seats, the cluster of representative persons, headed by
Premier Parkes, with most of his ordei's on, and
Mayor Burdekin in black and scarlet, did not
exactly impress one as a bi'illiant assemblage. The
One or two hired-carriage loads of M.’sL.A., for
instance, suggested a returning auction-sale crowd, and
from first to last the parade of local Majesty' out in its
best to back the Governor up was reminiscent of a third-
class “ burying ’’—minus the mutes. There Were very
few women in the cort&ge which followed H.E. to
Government Hcttse. The Bishop brought his chaperone
and the Misses Smith (all in deep black, of course), and in
another carriage was Lady Alice Shaw-Stewart (a sort of
codsin-gennan-hy-marriage to Lady J.), who also wore
black, with beaded mantelet te and a Diatronish black
-bonnet, looking in Her sombre attire like the principal
lady-motirner.
Lord Jersey is by no means unlike his portrait as pub-
lished by The Bulletin. He is a sinall and fragile gen-
tleman, with cricket-bat neck ami shoulders and noticeable
ears. He wears his damp, ginger hair combed eranewise
over his globe-shaped cerebrum ; and his Roman nose-
well, he obviously' suffers from indigestion. He wore a
seedy' blue coat, and held his three-cornered black hat
edged with fur in his hand (as he “ acknowledged ” the
strange murmurs with which the unemployed cheered
him on his course) in a way which suggested the malady
he was said to suffer from en voyage, and gave a funny
little nod from right to left. Opposite him in the carriage
sat the strawberry-blonde Earl of Ancrum, A.D.C., whose
scarlet uniform lit Up with gold cords corrected the
warmth of his personal coloring, while the Übiquitous
cyclorama-man supplied the necessary “white horse”
which everybody began to look for as the train went by.
The swearing-in ceremony at Government House, which
has been “renovated” in barber's shop style for the brand-
new vice-regality (the most noticeable changes being that
Lady Carington’s boudoir has been metamorphosed into a
billiard-room, and that a draughty Swiss chalet is in
course of erection in the West garden for the “staff” to
yarn in during business hours—thereby enlarging the
house-proper by five rooms) took place in the hall, a
number of ladies having seats on the stair-case while as
many people as could crowd into the passage formed a
neck-straining back ground in the doorway. The cere-
monies Were short and serious. Lord Jersey took his
three oaths in a nervous voice quite inaudible on the top
stair, and after Sir Alfred Stephen had introduced Mrs.
“ Seppy ” Stephen, the Misses Stephen, &c., &c., &c., to
the Earl he was allowed to escape to his private apart-
ments, and a great lull occurred until after luncheon,
when the levee came on and passed off as usual.
The suite of the Earl of Jersey really consists of two
nice young men, notwithstanding the length (on paper)
of his retinue. Mr. George J. Goschen,private secretary,
and the Hon. Rupert Leigh, A.D.C. (to whom for motives
of space ■The Bulletin will hereafter allude as Brother
Rupert No. 2) are strictly for “use,” while the gilded
Earl of Ancrum and the unphonetic Captain Cholmondeley
in their ornamental capacity as superfluous aides-de-camp
are only for “polishing.” And don’t you forget it.
At the Criterion, Miss Maud Williamson plays Brenda
Musgrave—a beagle-like charmer who is perpetually after
scalps—in a variety of frocks, the most edifying being a
black broche satin with frontal drape of jetted net, lace
elbow sleeves and regimental scarf from left shoulder to
right hip—a few red roses on the bodice to relieve the
general gloom. But Pattie Browne, as Nora Fitzgerald
(with a much more subdued Italian accent than she
favoured Melbourne with, by-the-by), absorbs all the
frocking interest. In the first acts she sports a
primrose - yellow lace striped lustre the stripes
going harrel-hoop-wise about the round skirt, bodice
of yellow guipure lace with high gigot sleeves,
pancake hat with yellow ribbons and what’s-o’clock
trimming. In 2nd act a pink silk dinner-gown with
scarves of pale-pink erfipe, the skirt and bodice trimmed
w ith gold galon. Brenda Gibson—as Lettice Vane—wears
white brochii bodice and train, the jupe made in the
window-blind style, the puffings held down by white satin
panels finished with pearl ornaments ; while Fanny Enson
resurrects pale-blue silk chiffon and silver passementerie
very effectively. In the last act Pattie wears seal-brown
cashmere with sleeves and side panel of velvet en suite,
dainty toque hat in tomato-red velvet, black jet and
blackbirds.
Apropos to the Criterion, it is most interesting to see
how trickily the only Tithe radge fools about with his
“stage-business” cigar, making believe to enjoy the
fragrant weed. I am told that Mr. Titberadge can no
more get through a Havanna than he could make the
gallery believe he is an angel—nevertheless, nobody ever
dreams that he can’t smoke.
Spurred on by a shattered tympanum, your Sappho
raises her indignant voice against the various theatrical
orchestras— especially in the smaller theatres—which are
getting less and less soothing every evening. In one
playhouse the interval harmony, provided to keep the
women from wondering how much longer James will he
out talking to Smith, sounds like a fat elephant climbing
over a quantity of gongs, and in another haunt of Thespis
the melody doesn’t softly steal over yon, as it were, but
comes with a pelt and a jump as if the musicians were
throwing it at the audience. <t.
Have you ever been struck by the oddness of the
popular scepticism in regard to the ability of an actress
to, play upon the piano-? “ She’s not playing,” is the
invariable whisper when a stage sojo is struck up, “ it’s
somebody at the wings,” though why all the musical
genius should be concentrated there, isn’t explained.
No matter how stupidly simple the tune may he, the
actress never obtains the credit of performing it—the
talented pianist “ behind ” gets all that. But, as a matter
of fact, most actresses play what is termed “a little”—
quite enough to trickle about over the ivories in the
drawing-room scene, while the jenn premier sentimen-
talizes in the centre.
Mayoress Burdekin left Sydney for Bowral on 14th inst.
where she purposes staying a week. While at Bowral
Mrs. Burdekin was present at the Bong Bong picnic races
on 15th and 10th inst—.quite fashionable sporting fixtures
in the recuperating district.
There is_no rest for the sassiety person in season or out
of it, and the reason is that the sassiety person is generally
a person who cannot be left to its ow n devices, because it
hasn’t got any, and therefore it hugs its chains whether
at Potts Point or Mount Victoria, though dll the time
pettishly complaining of the very conditions it is most
anxious to preserve! Take any “furnished mountain
cottage ’’-region—like Blackhcath. Its denizens swarm
upon it avowedly for “ rest ” and “ quiet,” but they don’t
want such hUm-drum things, else why keep up a metro-
politan round of dissipation ? A lady recently abandoned
the situation in disgust and came back to town, where
matters social are quiet as the grave—and actually' bene-
fited by it. She was getting quite fagged out with
“calls” and dinner parties and dances—all the regular
season tricks in the flaming heat of January—and found
that the deserted metropolis presented more features of
absolute rest than the “tourist district” does in the
swing of the summer season. No wonder that the dis-
heartened paterfamilias gazes dismally at the sallow, run-
down faces of mamma and the girls, after he hits paid top
prices for their recuperation ! ■
I wonder whether the reason is that the average woman
cannot read the plain legend, “ Foot-passengers keep to
tlie right,” that she almost invariably stems the tide of
pedestrians on our main streets ? With her parasol at the
angle of a ship’s spar she worries up or down the wrong
side of the kerb in a series of diagonal dodges suggestive
of setting to partnei's, and the flying business-men can
never allow for her tackings being regular. For if
he goes left she trips him up with a pained expression
about her eyebrows, and if he goes right she suddenly
makes up her mind to get round him that way, and
another embarrassing block occurs. If she worried her
anti-bylaw.in a tolerably' straight line, you eould make
latitudinal allowances for her, but it is that erratic side-
tack which demoralises a whole paveinent-load of people
who have to pull up short sometimes in the shape of the
letter C to avoid sending her spinning into the gutter.
Queensland is ahead in the matter of Servants’ Unions >
One of which was inaugurated at Blackall by five Bridgets
and a chairwoman. Rates of wages were agreed upon,
and among others the flowery reg’latioh that the down-
trodden creature in the plush jacket should have every
Sunday afternoon from three to five, aftd every alternate
Sunday night from eight to ten, together with one hotir
two afternoons a week, and three evenings a w’eek from
eight to ten. Wages were treated equally lavishly by the
meeting, and if the Union doesn’t prove a fizzle the
Bananaland “ Mum ” will have to pay her housemaid I5S-
a week, her nurse-girl 125., afid her lady cook not less
than two pounds !
A very pretty wedding was that of Miss Pope, Of
“ Dapovispe,” Ashfleld, to Mr. A. A. Boyd, which took
place at Summer Hill on 14th inst. The bride looked her
nicest in white ottoman silk, trimmed with the new
flsher’s-net silk fringe, and was attended by One maid

was charmingly decorated with beautiful ferns), and at

bride’s going-away gown was a pale-grey and priinrose
delaine, with toque hat of black and gold lace and creatn
roses. On their return to Sydney the pair will issue
invitations for a large dance.
People who saw' how the Cat jumped in certain circles,
are now wondering whether an imminent eminent lady
Personage will show as much antagonism to a local she-
Magnate as did her predecessor. Some said it was the
green-eyed monster—on account, perhaps, of better gown-
ing, for jealousy generally rests on a petty basis—others
averred that it was simply the wild longing to snub which
possesses even the most charming women when confronted
by a personality hostile to them. There never was the
faintest pretence of the entente cordiale between these
ladies, and it now remains to be seen whether personal
aversion or official etiquette was at the bottom of it.

Madame Weigall’s Fashion Journal for January is
principally remarkable for a bright orange-coloured cover
which was not there of yore, for the styles are the same
as they have been all summer and there is nothing from
first to last which the amateur dressmaker would feel
even the faintest thrill of pleasure in constructing. The
journal draws attention to variety in riding-apparel, and
coolly states that “linen skirts, covert coats, leather
belts, and straw sailor hats can be worn with a riding
skirt.” Of course-certainly, only the effect of a covert
coat when the wearer is going at a gallop does not soothe,
and a linen skirt filled with wind is not so graceful as a
well-fitting habit bodice, nor is it—even though Lady
Scott adopted it once or twice—considered good form on
horseback.
A crowded house—not only composed of youngsters
attended the “Dick Whittington” matinee at Her
Majesty’s on Wednesday last. Nearly every one of
Handsome George’s ballet-girls high kicks in a dark wig,
and it is rather a relief. One does get tired sometimes of
the evergreen blonde flossy Tottie of the front row.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

One of the most sensational cases in
Divorce at the next N.S.W. sittings will be a re-
hearing of a case tried not long ago. On this
occasion the respondent’s attorney and two of his
clerks are joined as co-respondents.
Two standard law books—“ Walker on
Executors” and “Simpson on Infants”—now
published in second editions, claim parentage from
two leading members of the N.S.W. Equity Bar.
The legal reviews speak well of them.
It is said that Queen Victoria has never
lost a penny by unwise speculation. The amount
of real estate she owns in London is simply
enormous. One block alone is said to include
both sides of the Quadrant and Regent-street,
from Piccadilly circus to Oxford-street—the most
valuable property in the kingdom.

In the manifesto of the Amalgamated
Shearers’ Union it is forcibly pointed out that the
Pastoralists’ Union demand that the shearers
shall work, under their rules only, with blacklegs,
scabs, Chinamen, and non-unionists, and that,
carried away by their partial success in connection
with the maritime strike, the pastoralists now
attempt to coerce the 28,000 men who comprise the
Shearers’ Union into a return to the position
wherein each, considered as a unit, was practically
powerless to resist the greed or tyranny of
employers.
It is obviously the intention of the Aus-
tralian associated employers to smash utterly the
Australian trades-unions. £lOO,OOO is to be raised
in Melbourne as the nucleus of a political fighting
fund tuphemistically called “The Employers’
Mutual Defence,Association.” Towards this one
banking institution has given £5OOO, a firm of
wool brokers £lOOO, and there are many one
hundred and two hundred-pound subscriptions.
The workers must now organise, establish the One
Man One Vote system, and storm all the Legisla-
tures.

“The Middleman” is a play of some
merit sociologically considered, but chiefly in-
teresting because of the dramatic power of the
central acts (the first and last acts are weak) and
its strong contrasts in characterisation. It takes
an actor of more than ordinary power to hold the
stage by himself, as does Charles Cartwright in
Cyrus Blenkarn during long scenes on lines that,
from a feebler source, would quickly weary an
audience. The actor’s impersonation of the
genius, robbed of money, fame, daughter; faint,
yet pursuing his ideal, regardless of all else, is
vivid, convincing, and one of the finest things, in
its way, Sydney has seen for a long time. Olga
Nethersole, as Mary, has a comparatively small
part, which she lifts out of insignificance by
clear enunciation, fine facial expression, and her
ability to simulate tears in the voice. Edward
Sass, as the Middleman, a character built, as the
D.T. suggests, somewhat on the lines of Consul
Bernick in Ibsen’s “Pillars of Society,” gets a
good show and makes the most of it. The Jesse
Pegg of Hans Phillips is, perhaps, the best piece
of work he has shown for a long time, anyhow, it
is a very good performance. Nellie Lyons is all
right as Nancy, and “Bill” Leake, safe as a
church, eagerly seizes on the show to air his in-
destructible white “spats” as Batty Todd. William
evidently believes in the old adage, “Keep
your head cool and your feet hot,” for
he never wears a hat by any chance at
any time, no matter where the scene is laid.
Harold Russell is effective in a minor part; so is
Alice Deorwyn and the only dialect part in the
play, old Danksj is tottered and jabbered through
in good style by Charles Westmacott. We had
nearly forgotten the Captain Chandler, a manly,
broad rendering of a small but important part,
. by Harry Jewett, who is as popular here as round
the corner. “ The Middleman ” has caught on so
well that the season has been extended from six to
eight weeks. Covernor Jersey goes there to-night
(Thursday).
* * * ' *
A second hearing of “Harvest” discloses
many fine lines obscured by heaps of melodramatic
high falutin, and several strong situations
weakened by the tags the author has thought
necessary to put in the mouth of the heroine to
justify his selection of “Harvest” as a title.
Little Brenda Gibson is to be congratulated on the
tenderness and sweet freshness of her Lettice ; it
is her best performance up to date, though some-
what on the lines of “Sweet Lavender.” “ Dot”
Boucioault is laid up—seriously ill, in fact—with
repressed gout, and Prompter MTntyre plays
Babble Brook in an automatic fashion. By the
way, why do they offend the artistic eye and
spoil the beautiful gleh-scene by sticking gaudy,
large-bloomed flowers in the crevices of rocks
whore such flowers never grew since time was?
“ Comrades ”~a play by Brandon Thomas, pro-
duced some time ago at the old Bijou—follows,
date uncertain, owing to Boucy’s illness.

When Governor Jersey visited Her
Majesty’s on Monday evening, the Damnational
Anthem, to coin a term of opprobrium, was sung
by the company. How this theatre snared Jersey
first is not known. Manager Macdonald says the
overtures came from Government House. If so,
it is to be presumed that the revered title of the
building took the vice-regal fancy. Jersey, how-
ever, wasn’t a big draw ; he didn’t fill the circle,
while the rest of the house was crammed-with
people who came to see Maggie Moore. Business
continues brisk, and with the patronage of a fresh
Governor every week it might run' till Boxing-
Night. Lacking that, “ Dick ” looks like running
for another six weeks. The Wednesday matinees
are a pronounced success.
* * * *
Despite the lovely scenery, gorgeous
dresses, well-drilled marches, and the introduction
of live horses and a teal coach, “Cinderella” is
not such a big thing as was anticipated, and her
old glass slipper will be chucked out into the lane
on the 31st, with the empty porter-bottles, to
make way for the evergreen Grattan Riggs, who,
it was rumoured, would put up “Ten Drinks in a
Bar-room,” by Harry Ridout, but will play
“ Arrah-na-Pogue ” instead for six nights, fol-
lowed by “ The Irish Detective,” an entirely new
play, written, it is whispered, by Cardinal Moran
and Parnell. The Riggs season over, Bland Holt
comes along with “Master and Man,” which gives
us a show of seeing Arthur Lawrence at his best
as Humpy Logan.

* * * *
The present “ Pirates of Renzance ” per-
formance, at Melbourne Princess’s, is rather
better, taking one consideration with another,
than some previous revivals of the same opera at
the same house. Neither Gilbert nor Sullivan
appears at his best in “ The Pi rales,” the story
being frequently strained in its humour, and the
music of a more old-gothic-cathedral order than
usual. Probably the funniest feature of the show
on Saturday night was the seriously “loyal”
reception of a climax in which Gilbert ridicules
loyalty. The victorious pirates fall into an osm
tatious tremble and lay down their arms when the


sergeant of police desires them, in the Queen’s
name, to surrender. After which the Pirate King
sticks the pole of the Union Jack against his
bottom rib and warbles “Rule Britannia,” whilst
an allegorical vision of that apocryphal female
bossing the deep blue sea is disclosed to view at
the back of the stage, Even W.S.G. has never
devised a broader satire than this same, yet it was
far too subtle for the dress-circle and reserved
stalls at the Princess’s. The author should have
been there—ah ! that he should—to see the salt of
the Marvellous City rising from their seats to
cheer the sentiment that “Britons never will be
slaves ” sung by a mock buccaneer who, “ with all
his faults, dearly loves his Queen.” It was touch-
and-go whether the whole evening-dressed portion
of the audience wouldn’t strike up the National
Anthem and dissolve into tears of idiotic joy.
Miss Frances Saville’s first appearance as Mabel
gave further proof that “the Firm” have found
(or, rather, brought back to the operatic stage) a
prima-donna capable of taking Nellie Stewart’s
place in characters where dash and abandon are
not essential. The new lady’s voice and appear-
ance are alike sweet and graceful, and she has a
charm of manner sufficient to carry her fairly
through the part. Her ‘ ‘ form ” all round strikes
one as being much about the same as Marion
Hood’s. Howard Yernon is the Major-General
of yore, with a little more music in his voice than
he had a year or two back, and Ryley, as com-
pared with his ponderous predecessor, Marnook,
makes a good Pirate King. Leumane has dropped
into a way of taking his breath at awkward places,
and he doesn’t pair well with the majestic Mabel,
on account of not being built that way. Miss
Elsie Cameron looms intolerably large as Ruth,
but the agreeable May Pollard still struggles to
talk like Essie Jenyns without satisfactory results,
and the Sergeant of Police gets several encores for
his song because he can’t well avoid them. With
Elton, in “ Charity Begins at Home ” as an after-
piece, the present bill should boom until
‘ lolanthe ” is ready.

Nellie Stewart’s farewell week at the
Opera House started with “ Boccaccio ” on Satur-
day, when the house was no fuller than might
have been expected. To-night (Thursday) and
to-morrow “Paul Jones” will be put up for the
last times, and then the most capable, or certainly
the most suitably-cast company that have ever
appeared in a series of light operas in Australia
vtill be broken up and done for, collectively speak-
ing. About the artistic success of the Nellie
Stewart shows there can be no two opinions.
Everything possible has been accomplished, with
words that were second-rate or behind the times,
sometimes both, and it may be taken for granted
that the fair Ellen’s “ good-bye ” —presuming she
makes a speech will be spoken to a monstrous
big audience, full of hearty good wishes that she
will make a big hit in London and then return as
soon as possible to the old folks at home. Which
we are the old folks at home.

At the Alexandra they seem to be doing
good business, considering wind and weather, with
“ The Workman,” and if the days of that ’orny
’anded toiler are not long in the land he must
attribute any abbreviation to his unfilial attitude
towards an ancient but unvenerable father. The
law of Moses takes no discount off honour due to
a parent who fills his son’s family circle with
fumes of alcohol, and stirs his tea with a red
herring, nor did the renowned Hebrew consider
how much harder it is to swear to a father than
swear at him. The Commandment stands out
distinctly against a background of bygone ages,
so the laziest old ruin that ever got drunk at his
alleged progeny’s expense can’t be put outside on
the mat with safety. John Tressider doesn’t go
quite as far as this, but he betrays symptoms of
dissatisfaction when the author of his being comes
up from the country by excursion train and loses the
return ticket, and John’s subsequent misfortunes
are doubtless owing to the fact that he wants to
let his old man roost among the rats in a back
cupboard. “The Workman” improves on ac-
quaintance. A rustic song and dance in the first
act deserve mention, and Dampier’s conception of
the miserable old sponge, well set off by a typi-
cally tough and virtuous British matron, gets
funnier every night. Miss Lily, likewise, shows
a disposition to talk in a higher key, and her
sorrow for the loss of her sawdust baby is
genuinely pathetic. But then, few young spinsters
have buried, or been separated from, so many
stage children as Lily. Kingdom Come must be
pretty nigh full of her lost little treasures.

The Royal pantomime goes on the same
as ever. Some remarks in last issue; concerning
a certain style of acrobatic dancing have been
misunderstood to reflect upon the cleverness of
Franks and Marion. On the contrary, the par.
recognised the awful dexterity and hard thinking
lavished upon the performance in question, but
questioned whether it was aesthetically satisfac-
tory, or something, to the lofty souls of people
who write for papers, especially sixpenny papers.
There is nothing else in particular to say about
the pantomime, except that it is no_ business of
somebody if somebody else’s dressing-room is
loaded up with admirers, or, if it is, somebody
ought to know better. And which somebody we
jan’t say for certain.

Harry St. Maur holds a real good thing in
“Brighton,” the Criterion comedy which has
recently been produced at the Adelaide Royal with
more than average success. “ Brighton,” is by
Bronson Howard, we believe, and is known in
America as “ Saratoga ” ; its author, if we err not,
is Charles Wyndham’s son-in-law. Anyhow,
they’re related. It is wonderful what spicy morsels
are gobbled up and easily digested in the city of
tabernacles. The comedy affords Mr. St. Maur
ample scope to show his abilities, George Carey
shines in a character sketch of a deaf old gentle-
man, while Florence Seymour and Rosa Conroy
lend capable assistance and air the contents of an
apparently inexhaustible wardrobe, “ Queen's
Evidence ” will be turned on next. The Taylor-
Carrington Company are doing very well at
Garner’s Theatre, where “Rip Van Winkle ” is
walking round.
* 4)4 4)4 4(4
Even the fashionable actor is liable at
times to find a dead fly in his cup of bliss. For
instance, that too beautiful gondolier, Mr. Charles
Ryley, hasstill to escape from the theatre by devious
ways, because it has become the fashion to fall in
love with him, and, as a rule, there is a blonde
waiting to make his acquaintance—by violence, if
necessary—at the stage-door, and two brunettes
lying for him at the refreshment-room entrance;
and a gold-washed seraph at the corner with a
bouquet, who wants to state that she will be his
forevermore. And as Mr. Ryley doesn’t want
any seraph—or, at least, has no use for six of
them—he generally comes down stealthily, and
reconnoitres for a moment, and then bolts across
the street and runs for it. The other night, how-
ever, a lady fair of elevated lineage blocked him
on the stairs, and when he fled up into the re-
freshment-room she followed, and then people
were surprised to see an eminent baritone double
himself up underneath the counter just as a
female, with love shining in her eye, burst in at
the door. At times like these even the most
beautiful of gondoliers would almost rather be a
plain bald potato-dealer with a lame eye.

Writers who question the immense value
of acting experience to dramatic authors are apt
to forget that the most successful of modern play-
wrights have had some practical knowledge of
stage business before they made their names.
Boucicault was an actor in a small way, even
previous to the advent of “London Assurance.”
Tom Robertson came of theatrical people, his
sister, Madge, having been an “ infant pheno-
menon ” years before “ Society ” lifted her
brother out of poverty and obscurity. H. J.
Byron wrote several of his “fattest” easy parts
for himself, and Pinero’s first effort —a pretty
little, one-act comedy—was produced at the
Lyceum whilst he was still a member of Irving’s
company. In fact, Pinero played the leading
character, and did it uncommonly.well. Of “ un-
professional ” dramatists Charles Reade, W. S.
Gilbert, Buchanan, and G. R. Sims may be
instanced to show that whereas it takes (speaking
generally) a first-rate outsider to write a really
successful play, the actor-author can write one as
good, or better, without having any pretensions
to turn out novels, poems, Bab Ballads, or even
“Mustard and Cress” of sufficient excellence to
bring them celebrity. Sketches by Byron and
Pinero have appeared in theatrical publications,
and were found to be very colourless and soft. But
someone asks where do H. A. Jones, Henry
Hamilton and scores of others, neither actors nor
thoroughgoing journalists, come in ?
4)4 * * 414
The latest thing in French dramas is an
interesting little affair which will probably be
“adapted” into English at an early date, and
passed off as something which has been festering in
the author’s intelligence ever since the days of his
infancy. The plot is simple and full of pcetry.
The hero has a wife, as he is intensely indifferent
to the lady she bestows the haggard engine which
she terras her heart upon a male friend, and they
are both found out. Then the friend and the
husband expose themselves, and catch a bad cold
apiece, through fighting a duel, and the friend
undergoes considerable damage. When he gets
out of the hospital he makes an honourable pro-
posal to the effect that a divorce shall be obtained,
in which case he is willing to marry the bone of
contention. Just here it dawns on the husband,
who has hitherto taken matters with a profound
unaffeetionate calm, that the lady must be more
attractive than he imagined, else the other man
would not be so anxious to possess her, and he
falls in love officially with his own wife on the
spot. It is presumed that they are blessed with
14 children after this, including four pairs of
twins and one red-headed infant of deficient in-
telligence, and that they live happily and die
universally respected. Also, it is generally sup-
posed that the friend falls away from the paths of
rectitude, and is ultimately found dead in a horse-
trough.
4(4 * * *
Luscombe Searelle, now on his way to
Australia, has just been elected a member of the
Savage Club, He only got in by “ the skin of
his teeth ” though, his_ name having previously
been “sent back for inquiry.” It was. alleged
against him that when he had made such a
ghastly failure with the “ Wreck of the Pinafore ”
in London a few years ago, he left his crew
stranded. Not only has this been found to be
utterly untrue, but Searelle has, as a matter of fact,
since his return from South Africa paid all his
old debts with 10 per cent, interest. The real
opposition to him at the Savage, says a Bulletin
correspondent, came from A. W. Pinero and
W. S. Gilbert, the latter of whom bears malice
on account of the Searellian travesty of “Pina-
fore,”
At Poverty Point.
A recent addition to the roll of the theatrical dead is
the name of Harry Ashley, a rare good comedian in his
day. London papers seem to speak of him as having
been almost unknown prior to joining Charles Wynd-
ham’s company at the Criterion, but years before that he
had made his mark at the Adelphi, In a one-act drama
called “ Smoke’’—with Mr, and Mrs. John Billington and
Miss Furtado for colleagues—Ashley used to set the house
in a roar. And does anyone call to mind young Violet
Cameron singing “ Don’t you remember, dear old Dad?”
in the original English production of “Madame Favart,”
at the Strand Theatre ? Well, Ashley was the dear old
Dad. It was in that first run of the opera that Marius
hugged and kissed Florence St. John with an ardour
which warmed the whole house. Nothing so genuinely
amorous has been witnessed on the stage since those days,
when Florence was not yet divorced from her husband —
a second-rate baritone known as St, James—and Marius
was wooing her with the scorching fury of a sirocco in
full blast. Later on, when Marius had married his
“Sinjin” in sober earnest, the pair took the Avenue
Theatre and revived “Madame Favart”- —what a change
was there! AH the life had departed from the little
Frenchman’s stage embrace—no longer did he go for Flo.
like a famished schoolboy for a ripe, red apple, And she,
pale and apathetic, accepted her husband’s weak imita-
tion of kisses with an indifference which made the present
writer a cynic thenceforth and for ever. This, however,
has little to do with poor Harry Ashley— only “ poor” by
courtesy, because he happens to be dead.
Macready was at a fashionable dinner-table, when a lady
referred to the lower classes. “Never use that expres-
sion,” said he. “ Call them the poorer classes,”
Judic, the celebrated French variety actress, has
married her barber.

A Parisian paper recently started a rumour that Adelina
Patti had died. But Adelina, according to all accounts,
is in a fair way to live for ever. She eats, drinks, sleeps,
and takes oatmeal baths with the monotonous regularity
of a machine.
