Mrs. Agnes Robertson Boucioault’s character of her
husband, in the Weekly Scotsman, is so lustrous and
enthusiastic that one can only wonder they ever had a
vestige of quarrel—De Mortuis, &c., with woman’s logic
all over, God bless her ! Bouoicault, under Lardner’s
auspices, began as an engineer, and forwarded such a
clever scheme for London drainage that he would have
got a good billet had he not been so young, and the world
would never have seen the “Colleen Bawn.” One of the
brewer Guinness’s, a relative, adopted him, and sent him
to study, but he only studied little suppers, wherefore he
was cut off even shillingless. So gentle he was, and kind,
says good Mrs. Boucicault. She is still the Fairy Star, as
they used to call her in the States.
Every old actor can write- one good chapter of reminis-
cence, and no more. He collars you, like Ye Ancient
Mariner, and you go round saying to your friends, “ Good
Lord, why doesn’t Musty’ publish his reminiscences?”
Thereafter you are Musty’s victim, and the Mariner with
the glazed eye is a fool to him.
Nellie Stewart has been confiding in a Melbourne inter-
viewer. Father and mother, like most professionals,
were at first all against her going on the stage, but she
wouldn’t win the first prizes at school unless allowed to
play Prince Prettipet in the Pantomime, or something
like that. On- her last trip to England pa and raa would
only let her stay a year, but she learnt extra voice-pro-
duction from Randegger r and Letty Lind dancing from
D’Auban. Now’ she swoops on London—where all her
rivals are stale. Her own voice wants a rest badly.
In Westland Marston’s new theatrical memoirs there is
a passage about Adelaide Neilson. She married Mr. Lee,
a clergyman’s son, and the young actress went to the
parson’s house, where she was entertained better than
Miss Fortescue was by Lord and Lady Cairns. There is a
touching church anefstage guild picture, of Miss Neilson
the centre of a circle of parsons, at Mr. Lee’s, looking at
her as if she were a curious insect. So she was. She lay
some hours unrecognised in the Paris Morgue, after
which there was a squabble by a lot of bullies for her
money.
Charles Kean had an appetite for praise scarcely
equalled even amid actors, which is saying a lot, and you
will fancy you have witnessed something like the follow-
ing nearer home. After the first representation of
Lovell’s “Wife’s Secret” Mr. Lovell went round to
Kean’s dressing-room and gave him an enthusiastic
eulogy of his wife’s acting in the piece. Kean then
opened his mouth wide for something about himself,
but says Lovell, “ I never praise people to their faces.”
“Oh,” say’s Kean, “my dear fellow, my wife is in the
manager’s'room. Go and give her a full account.”

During a rehearsal of “Antony and Cleopatra” at
London ITincess’s in the banquet-scene the boards gave
way and 58 supers (not suppers) disappeared slowly into
the depths, then the scene pillars, like those in the
Philistines’ temple, collapsed. No one was hurt, and the
effect was said to surpass that of the earthquake in
Wilson Barrett’s “ Claudian.”

According to latest London advices, Harry Payne was
not to appear in the harlequinade at Drury Lane this
Xmas. Poor old Harry, the last survivor of the famous
Payne family, has “ clowned ” at Covent Garden or Drury
Lane for the last 30 years or so. But it was time he went.
The spectacle of a grey-headed man fooling with sawdust
babies, whilst his grandchildren sat in the pit and yawned
their contempt, was more pathetic than improving.
This is news. The Pall Mall Gazette seriously says
“Mr. Parnell is well-known in Australia as a dramatic
author. For more than five years a play, entitled
‘Shamrock Green: By Charles Stewart Parnell, Esq.,
M.P.,’ has enjoyed prodigious favour amongst provincial
audiences in the colonies. The lucky exclusive proprietor
of this piece boasts of having already netted £5OOO by it.
Its proprietor— an Irishman, by the way—has never yet
ventured to produce it in Melbourne or Sydney, doubtless
from a shrewd suspicion that the dramatic critics of these
capitals would want to know something more about its
history and antecedents than the bald announcement on
the playbills that ‘ Mr. Parnell wrote this play when a
young man at college.’ Provincial pressmen are not so
unpleasantly sceptical, and the Irish men and women of
the interior towns consider it a patriotic duty to roll up
in their hundreds to see ‘ Mr. Parnell’s play.’ ”

One of the London actresses is distressed because she

on this?”
Mrs. Agnes Robertson-Boucicault, the famous drama-
tist’s first and legal wife and mother of his family,
writes some reminiscences of him, in the Scotsman.
Dion was a regular Dumas in his carelessness about
money, speculating more than spending, and appeared
to require the excitement of an everlasting Monte
Carlo. He was nominally Church of England, but
reckoned himself a Roman Catholic at last, though his
wife calls him a Unitarian. Like Victor Hugo, he firmly
believed in a God, and had a remarkably intimate know-
ledge of the Bible.
It is settled, on the authority of Mrs. Potter, Sara
Bernhardt, and Mrs. Langtry, that Cleopatra wore a
gorgeous teapot on her head, with the spout forward, em-
blematic of Isis, Osiris, and Horus. But for the real
acting of Cleopatra, you have to go back to Miss Glynn,
whose Antony, Henry Lorraine, takes a humble part in
Mrs. Langtry’s Cleopatra. Bet you he goes up to her,
with tears in his eyes, grasps both her hands, and says,
“ Madam, you have shattered my idol! ” Whereupon she
stands a pint. Old Jack Ryder, Macready’s associate, did
this to Edwin Booth, in London, over Richelieu.
That shapely young soubrette, Sophie Harris, now resid-
ing with Mr. and Mrs. Pemberton Willard at Randwick was
recently sleeping the dreamless and peaceful slumbers of
the innocent at midday, after the cares of pantomime,
when two burglars entered her room and carried off 19
tram tickets, three postage stamps, silver nail-cleaner,
gold ring, stud, key, compass, 205., and a purse. Sophie
never stirred a toe-nail while they rummaged her duds,
but when they started to break open the Willards’boxes in
an adjoining room she softly cleared out for assistance*
and following the burglars in a cab had them arrested.
Sophie, who is real grit, says she wasn’t afeard. But we
notice it changed the colour of her hair.

Nigger-driving in
Queensland.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
Apropos to the system of “ hiring ”
kanakas to slave* for a term of years among
the sugar-canes of the tropical Korth, which
system came to an end by law on the last
day of the year that lias just closed, it may be
interesting to recount a few experiences. In
the year 1884, when the Queensland sugar-
industry was booming, I acted as chief
nigger-driver at Fairdale plantation, just below
the tropics. Fairdale was owned by a firm who
had made money during a long career of sheep-
farming in Victoria, and who, after much pro-
specting for land—sugar-lands at that period were
bringing fabulous prices—had purchased between
400 and 500 acres of sour open forest country
below flood-mark on the banks of the.Kooralin
River. Here by the moisture of the land and
extensive processes of manuring, they hoped in
time to reapagolden harvest through the medium
of the waving cane.
* * *
At the time of my advent to Fairdale the
plantation was in a fairly ship-shape condition.
The first crop of cane was rapidly maturing, and
the erection of a £60,000 manufacturing mill, em-
bracing all the latest devices, had just been com-
pleted. A large number of kanakas were already
on the spot, and 100 more were exacted to arrive
within a fortnight. Accordingly the haste and
bustle of preparation were everywhere apparent,
and the commencement of the crushing was fixed
for that day month.
Fairdale had five kanaka-overseers, of whom I
was chief. That is to say, I had the privilege
accorded me—over and above the wage emolument
of 255. per week—of living at the house, whereas
the other four had to “pig it,” together with 50
or 60 white labourers, horse-drivers, and what not,
in barracks which stood behind the mill, where
their bodily wants were supplied by a Chinese
cook and his half-dozen dusky satellites. The
only overseer of the bunch, myself included, who
presented any striking characteristics, was an
enormous Irishman named M‘Groarty, the field
overseer. His leading feature was brutality to
his black charges—brutality of the most wanton
and awful description. He wovdd kick a “boy”
if he did what he was told, he would kick him if
he didn t; he would kick or strike him with a
heavy horse-whip, whether he turned up early or
whether he turned up late. M'Groarty was cer-
tainly a wicked man with a heart as remorseless
and cruel as that of the arch-fiend, just the man
for a Bible-reading “Rescue” expedition into
Central Africa. But he got the work done.
* * #
Fairdale plantation represented a capital
investment of £lOO,OOO, which was expected to
yield an annual interest—inclusive of the risk and
personal labour of the partners—of 10 per cent.
The brothers Oldfield were what the world calls
good men, and two of them were Christians of the
most bigoted type, as were the two ladies who
presided over their household—all of them, how-
ever, were determined to make that interest.
And they would have done it, too, had sugar
stood firm at £36 per ton.
Whentheslave-ship, or rather “labour”schooner,
as she was facetiously termed in those parts,
arrived at the township with the extra hundred
“boys,” I was deputed to go into town and
receive and take them out to the plantation. I
found the ship moored in midstream, opposite the
Custom-house, and had no difficulty in getting
one of her boats to come and take me off. The
vessel was a long, low, rakish-looking schooner,
painted white with an encircling black riband—
the insignia of the Queensland slave—by courtesy,
Pacific Island labour-trade. On the quarter-deck
were grouped the captain, mate, and Government
agent, all drunk. The latter official is the one
tribute to humanity in connection with the trade
conceded by the Government—he accompanies
the ship to see after the black man’s interest. All
other interests are those of the indenting sugar-
planter, which are naturally against the unhappy
immigrant. But then the planter’s rum often
casts a glamour over the Government agent; this
one, I was told, had been drunk throughout the
voyage. And he looked it.
All the sober white people visible on board
the schooner were three local sugar-planters, the
Polynesian Inspector (a local Government official),
and myself, but there were 376 sober immigrants on
board the ship, all looking forward with the most
sanguine_hopes to the fame and fortune they were
sure awaited them in this new country. It was
easily evident from their demeanour that they had
longed for years for an opportunity to come, and
that they would at any time during their sojourn
in their native homes have bartered their best
wives and very choicest pigs for passages to
Queensland. The drafting of the labourers soon
began, and I found to my astonishment I was not
allowed to select the “ boys ” I wanted, but had,
according to law, to take a fair run of the herd,
after the manner of sheep-drafting. This, I may
explain, is a system which confers on each planta-
tion its fair proportion of the halt, the maimed,
and the blind who are regularly brought over in
these ships, and who never do a' day’s work from
the day they arrive to the day they depart, and
who not infrequently die soonafter'their arrival.
You sse, between the anxiety of the Islanders to
come, and the anxiety of the ship-master and
crew (under the “lay” or premium system) to
bring them, a large portion of the kanakas brought
over to Queensland are so diseased and maimed as
to be utterly unable to work. Well, after half-a-
day’s haggling with the Polynesian Insoector, and
the other local planters interested hi the ship-
ment, I got away to Fairdale in the evening witli
a hundred head, including men, women and
children. There were then some 270 dusky
labourers on the estate. A fortnight later, the
crushing began.
* # #
Ihe first few weeks of the crushing gave
M‘Groarty ample opportunity to exercise his
abilities in breaking in the recruits and he em-
ployed those abilities without stint. Being myself
in charge of the mill I saw very little of what
went on in the field, but heard everything. Three
kanakas, brutally injured by the fist and foot of
this modern Degree, were conveyed into the town
hospital during the first month of the crushing,
and three times M'Groarty appeared before the
local police-court charged, at the instance of the
Polynesian inspector, with occasioning actual
bodily harm to the “boys.” On each occasion he
was fined £5 and costs (the total sum being paid
by his employers), and the latter were notified by
the inspector, that on the next repetition of the
the offence he should insist on M'Groarty’s dis-
missal ; and that disregarded, would not allow
them to have any more labour from the islands.
The next offence was not long in coming and
M'Groarty had perforce to be parted with, not,
however, without many expressions of regret on the
part of his employers and a valedictory cheque for
£5O. I had almost forgotten to mention that on
Sunday the whole of the “ boys ” were sedulously
instructed in the Bible by the ladies of the home-
stead and one of the more piously-inclined of the
brothers. This, doubtless, was the ameliorating
influence which gave them fortitude to bear their
burdens during the week.
At length the fourth month of the crushing was
reached. The success of the manufacturing had
been phenomenal. Fairdale “firsts,” although
raw sugars, stood higher in the Southern markets
than any of the best refined. It was November,
and the mill was working double shifts in a vain
endeavour to get through before Christmas. The
summer heat was intense. The clouds of steam
laden with the fumes of boiling molasses which
HAPPY HOURS.
MRS. DRYNCKMORE (to her “alleged ” lord and master) : “This is a nice time to come
home, is it not?”

I come ’ome.”
polluted the air around the mill day and night,
were now supplemented by the ascending vapours
from thousands of tons of the rotting megass
which had been deposited in stacks all round the
mill, between the huts, and far out into the field.
There was no time to remove the waste products ;
every minute was needed to make sugar. And
so the cane, after it passed through the masti-
cating process of manufacture, was stacked here
and there in long, low ridges of decaying vegeta-
tion.
• * *
About the beginning of December a
“boy” was taken very ill. The overseer, ever
incredulous, beat him to his work that morning,
but the lad, with true nigger obstinacy, crawled
back to the hut and—died within twelve hours.
The same performance was repeated by individual
“ boys ”on the next day, and the next, and the
next, until, on the fifth day, when the morning
bell rang at 5.30 there were 25 “boys” short.
Things were getting serious ; the bosses must be
consulted. Accordingly, a conference took place
between Messrs. Oldfield and the overseers*
Efforts made to get the “boys’’out one by one
proved all in vain ; and so a messenger was sent to
town for a doctor. Dr. Staaffe was out when
the messenger arrived, but his servant promised
that he would come to Fairdale early in the after-
noon. He did so. He reached the mill at 4.30, but
seventeen of his intended patients had already
passed beyond his reach. He pronounced the
malady to be a complication of jungle and
malarial fever of the most virulent character, and
recommended that a hospital be fixed up, and that
the services of a medical man be permanently
secured. This was done, and the crushing
went on. The trucks which ran in the cane
ran out the dead bodies of the kanakas to
be buried in the trenches in the fields. Fif-
teen white men also died, but were interred
a little more in accordance with the forms of
orthodoxy. On 17th December the 270 coloured
labourers on the plantation had dwindled down to
' 90, and the mill was hung up for want of labour.
Rain was also pouring down steadily, day after
day, and it was evident that the wet season had
set in. I had fifteen pounds to draw, and deter-
mined to leave for the South.
* * *
Last February I revisited Fairdale and
ascertained that the Messrs. Oldfield were still
there. They had settled down to a steady,
bucolic kind of life; sugar had fallen the year
after they started, and they soon learnt that
it was not possible to sweat the interest of
the usurer out of the flesh and blood of the South
Set Islander. They had accordingly determined
to cut up the land into lots, and to let it
on easy terms to white men, who, with the labour
of their families and the aid of the improved
mechanical appliances now available, would grow
the cane. The proprietors would purchase and
manfacture it. This the Messrs. Oldfield assured
me was the only reasonable and only possible
solution of the black-labour difficulty.
Nigger-Driver.
Tail-end of a burst of loyalty from the
Melbourne D.T. :
Meanwhile, it may be as well to remark that if General
Booth does select Australia, with its millions of acres of
waste land, its need of population, and its ability to sup-
port them, as a theatre for his colonising scheme, not Mr.
Munro nor all the Australian Premiers and Parliaments
combined could prevent him from carrying out the inten-
tion. We may legislate for the exclusion of the Chinese
and convicts, but we cannot, even if we would, debar an
Englishman from settling on any portion of the British
Empire to which he chooses to travel.
Perhaps not. But, in reply, The Bulletin begs
to suggest that Sappho Smith, and the young
man who keeps the books downstairs, and the
gentleman who pays the salaries on Saturday, are
willing to unite at a crisis, and guarantee that, by
their joint efforts, at least one Englishman will
settle in Australia with considerable difficulty.
And if the other inhabitants of the country will
tackle the problem in the same spirit, the first
million Salvation converts will quit this portion
of the British Empire with unreasoning haste,
whether they are “debarred” by legislation or
■ not. The Bulletin has no precedent to offer,
-but it has an indignant lady with an umbrella,
which is worth a lot more than any precedent;
also, it is fitted with a keen sense of the undesir-
ableness of allowing a lot of square-shouldered,
scrofulous-complexioned, hollow-chested English
workhouse wrecks to come and spit the bacillus
tuberculosis all over our island.
A high-toned teetotaller from “ home ” —■
one Sir Morville Wraxall, Bart., states that he is
exceedingly pleased with the climate of Victoria
(which is more than some of us are), and thinks o!
settling down to temperance-work in Melbourne.
Music :
A Nolle Mission.
Good Sir Morville Wraxall,
You had better settle down
To reform the Jills and Jacks all
In that evil-smelling town;
For a little bird says Jill is
Scarcely better than her beau.
Who prefers a “shout” with Gillies
To a lecture by Munro.
Good Sir Morville Wraxall,
Rather “drinky ” in their way
Are some dames who show their backs all
Bare before us at the play;
Though you’ll hear my lady swear her
Pretty daughter sticks to tea,
There are times when haughty Sarah
Takes her sal volatile.
Good Sir Morville Wraxall,
You may well be looking sad,
For we grieve to say Toorak’s all
Going downward to the bad;
Thither baskets made of wicker
Are consigned, we understand,
Holding bottles full of liquor
For the rulers of the land.
Good Sir Morville Wraxall,
Go and wait upon a “ toff ”
With your figures and your fax all
Written down to rattle off :
Also tell his wife and daughter
That a titled person’s come
To explain how filtered water
Is superior to rum.
Good Sir Morville Wraxall,
Sure the uppah push have got
In their morals very lax all—
You must let ’em ’ave it ’ot:
Talk about the pit of Tophet
As your worthy brothers do—
Toffs will hearken to a prophet
When bis British blood is blue.
Good Sir Morville Wraxall,
Never mind plebeian Dick,
Who has a “ burst ” and whacks all
His relations with a stick.
By and bye he turns from “ tangle ”
And quite penitent he gets —
No, Sir Morville, you must angle
For the knights and baronets.
Ask Yourself.
Ask yourself these questions: In what con-
dition am I to-day? Am I perfectly sound in
body and in mind ? Am I strong and vigorous,
and is life a constant pleasure to me ? If you can
answer these questions affirmatively you are in-
deed lucky. If you cannot, you need assistance.
If you are tired, weak, weary, or despondent, you
need assistance. The trying time of the year is
approaching with its dangers to physical health.
Do not neglect a headache ; it is a sign that your
liver is deranged, and may lead to permanent
disease if not attended to. If you are troubled
with restless nights, neuralgia, indigestion, ner-
vousness, or constipation, don’t treat such positive
symptoms of approaching physical decline with
contempt, or bitterly will you rue your careless-
ness. Such invidious foes are more dangerous and
fatal than all the wars and famines that ever
eventuated, Mr. Charles Schott, of 94, King-
street, Newtown, N.S.W., writes as under, date
July 23rd : —“ I have much pleasure in testifying
to the efficiency of Clements Tonic in curing me
of congestion of the liver. For seven years I have
suffered from constant enlargement of the liver,
which caused me severe pain, and was due to a
sedentary life and want of exercise, my life being
almost insupportable. I was listless and irritable,
and at times had to consult doctors, and was at
one time laid on a sick bed ; but all to no purpose.
Suffering in this way for many years, I resorted
to the use of Clements Tonic, and after the use of
three bottles the swelling of the liver was reduced,
and with its use pain disappeared. After taking
five bottles I can now eat and drink anything,
and smoke my pipe without that continual irritable
and sleepy feeling resulting. I trust this letter
will lead similar sufferers to the use of Clements
Tonic, giving it a fair trial, not merely using one
bottle, and then discarding it, and I am satisfied
they will derive the same benefit.” Mr. James
Prentice, proprietor of the Newtown (N.S.W.)
Chronicle, has for some time been a most acute
sufferer from liver complaint, lassitude, indiges-
tion, &c., due to business care and close applica-
tion to work. He took Clements . Tonic —For
some time I have suffered considerably from lassi-
tude and indigestion, resulting from close atten-
tion to work. To obtain relief I tried all sorts of
so-called “cures,” but found none that gave me
any ease. I was at last induced to use Clements
Tonic, and having done so, regret not having
taken it sooner. After taking only two bottles
my lassitude vanished. I was able to attend to
my duties with my usual energy, and also to relish
my food and enjoy my rest, in a way that had
been unknown for some time. I shall always
recommend Clements Tonic, believing that it
only requires to be tried to be appreciated.

Goulburn Cathedial lb su, the Goulhurn
man whose rcd-Lic an 1 tombstone have made him
famous, is son of Captain Rossi, who was police-
magistrate at Sydney in 1825. There is a legend
that the elder Rossi was a witness in the case
against Queen Caroline.
Within the last 12 months three South
Australian members of Parliament have died
violent deaths. Ramsay, M.P., was burned to
death in a railway-train ; Cowan, M.P., was run
over at a railway crossing; and now Kelly, M.P.,
has been thrown out of a trap and killed.
The death of Bancroft, the American his-
torian, is reported by cable. He hoped to finish
his history this winter, but said to a friend, a few
weeks ago : “ I have laid my work upon the shelf
and can now only wait for the future summons.
Literary effort has passed beyond my powers. - ’
M. Montrond, a friend of Talleyrand’s,
went aboard an English man-of-war for safety
from the French Revolutionists. Said the cap-
tain, at dinner, “ I believe all Frenchmen are
scoundrels.” Replied Montrond: “ I believe all
Englishmen are gentlemen—save two or three.’
But one political appointment has been
made to the N.S.W. minor bench —that of Ex-
Solicitor-General Josephson, who, however, re-
signed the appointment ere pensions to minor
judges came about. But then the ex-judge is so
disgustingly rich that he can do without a
pension,
Mrs. Throshy, of Throsby Park, one of
the oldest natives of N.S.W., died last week,
aged 84. In 1824, when she was a girl of 17, she
married Mr. Charles Throsby at the Church of
St. Luke, Liverpool, N.S.W. She was the
daughter of Comissary Broughton, an old-time
Imperial official.
It is generally assumed that Bramwell
Booth, General Booth’s eldest son, will succeed to
the command of the Salvation Army, but the
British Weekly hears that such is not the case,
Bradlaugh’s health, it is cabled, is in a
most critical state.

has entered his 88th year. He is a native of

the bushranger, dead in fair stand-up fight.

travelling through America delivering his lectures
to raise the funds to enable her to erect a monu-
ment to his memory on San Miguel, a mountain
near the Californian town of San Diego.

sold his little library of choice books to raise
funds to take hiin to England in order to be
called to the Bar. Good prices were given, chiefly
by people who admired the little man’s pluck.

regret that he had not a formal chance to refuse.

bility.
Bachelor Jimmy Tyson, Australia’s most
successful squatter, has just entered his 68th year,
and is said to be worth six millions. Which is
probably near the truth, for James avoids, and
has all his life avoided, and profoundly mistrusted,
both wine and women.

One of the things which Stanley does not
mention in his lectures is that he served on both
sides in the American civil war, and was a re-
markably bad failure in each case. He was a
Confederate first, and was peacefully taken
prisoner in his first battle I and then he was a
Federal who didn’t go into any battle at all.

ate to all great self-advertisers, including Phmeas

Rochefort had spoken disparagingly of
Sara Bernhardt,whose (illegitimate) son, Maurice,
called and demanded retractation or reparation.
“My dear young man,” said Rochefort, cool as a
cucumber, ‘‘ I can’t fight you, yon know. ” “Yen
can’t fight me, sir! What do you mean?
“Why,” rejoined Rochefort, “for all we
know I might be your papa.” This story has
vaguely floated round for years, but having been
fathered it acquires new interest.
POLITICAL
POINTS.
There is a whisper that it is in contempla
tion to propose a retiring allowance to Sir Sam
Samuel, the N.S.W. Agent-General.
What has become of Parkes’ proposed
Australian Council, meeting in London, members
of which were to be elected by the Australian
legislatures ?
How is it that N.S.W. Minister of Justice
Gould denies the release from gaol of two women
whose release has been officially announced in the
Police Gazette?
The West Australian “ Six Families ” and
their friends are happy. Four out of the five
Ministers in the new Cabinet are-leading members
of that promising organisation. A Civil Service
Commission is not to be thought of under the cir-
cumstances.
South Australia recently appointed a Com-
mission to inquire into the best means of bringing
about Intercolonial Freetrade. It has just recom-
mended that the best way is by assimilating the
S.A. tariff with that of Victoria! Comment
would be cruel.
Rev. E. Griffith (Sir Samuel’s papa), of
Brisbane, is doing the advance-agent for General
Booth’s emigration show. “ The change of
heart” which the Yankee reformer will effect,
the rev. gentleman thinks, will render the London
unwashed the most desirable of colonists.
Why should Secretary Blaine, United
States, resent the reference of the Fisheries Ques-
tion to the legal tribunals of his own country ?
Can it be felt, as it was in Victoria over the Land
Act, that the Supreme Court cannot be trusted ?
All the Victorian land legislation was explicitly
defended by legislation against the Judges, with
an eye to Chief Justice Stawell’s enmity to the
people.
It is cabled that “the first batch of Jews
who are emigrating from Russia, in consequence
of persecution, have arrived in London to
fasten on the throats of the sweated London
tailors, and so on. Then the sweated London
tailors will, after being spiritually regenerated, be
transported to Australia by General Booth,
leaving room in England for another “batch” of
Russian Jews.
Says “ J.C.C.” in the Sydney Quarterly S
“Property is sacred say some, but so is Labour)
so is Poverty. All is sacred that is really human)
for all that is truly human has been drawn into
unity with God.” Now, poverty is not human, it
is clearly inhuman ; property ceases to be sacred
when it conflicts with what is human, has no
rights only duties, and is, broadly speaking, the
parent of the other inhumanity, poverty.
Now that black labour is at an end, the
Queensland “ cullud ” journals have turned to
the cheap continental article. This isn’t even
according to the law. of expediency, as the accom-
plished doom of the kanaka gave them a splendid
chance to “back down” decently and turn to
better ways. When cheap and nastly continental
labour fails, as it is bound to do, the journals that
pervert public opinion and hamper the progress
of the nation will assuredly fail too.
The German Emperor on school reform •
If the schools had done what they ought they should
have taken up the cudgels against Social Democracy of
their own accord, and the teachers should have so
brought up the rising generation-my contemporaries-
that they would have furnished me with material to work
with in the State in order to master the movement more
speedily.
As the Pall Mall puts it, he evidently considers
the nation merely an armed camp, and the school-
master a primary drill-sergeant.
In the House of Commons, Mr, Jennings
asked, from the Conservative benches ; “ Why
the Duke of St. Albans continued to receive
allowances for providing falconers and hawks and
pigeons, when, as a matter of fact, there arc
neither pigeons to feed the hawks nor falconers to
fly the hawks, nor hawks to be flown ? Charles
11. was a sanguine man,” said he, “but he never
dreamt that in the year 1890 the people of Eng-
land would still be paying £2OOO a year to the
descendants of Nell Gwynne.”
The Queensland Government has exempted
some 800 persons in minor positions from the
operation of the Superannuation Clauses of the
Civil Service Act, and these will have their con-
tribution money returned them with interest.
Which should be a hint to the N.S.W. Govern-
ment to return to its female public-sohool teachers
the four per cent, deducted from their earnings,
for not one woman in a hundred ever stays long
enough in the service to reach a pension. The
deduction of the percentage under these circum-
stances is a cruel and shameless robbery.
Just figure a career for the child-Queen of
the Netherlands somewhat based on that of such
beautiful Queens as Isabella and Christina in
Spain. Such ladies must have on hand a bully of
an O’Donnell, Narvaez, Espartero, or Serrano, for
they are like Catherine of Russia, except that
their brains are not to be found where looked for.
Consider the position of the present Regent Queen
of Spain, with a lot of fighting-cocks fluttering
round, trying to ensnare her. In Courts they
ignore human nature, which, driven out of the
palace door, comes in at the Erench window.
It is said that in the event of a general
election Parnell would return with 50 seats while
the seceders would have 25. The worst feature
of the disruption, for Home Rule, is that nearly
all the best men have left Parnell. Sexton, one
of the greatest orators living; Healy, the
organiser; Arthur O’Connor, a man who knows
more of Parliamentary practice than perhaps any
other man in the House ; “T. P.,” the best Irish
platform speaker; O’Brien and Dillon, the two
most popular of the Irish representatives; and
Justin McCarthy, a man of great literary attain-
ments and irreproachable character.
Dear Bulletin, —Parnell was unfortunate
in the selection of his candidate for the recent
Kilkenny election. Mr. Vincent Scully is one of
a family whose acts as Irish landlords have caused
as much cruelty and bloodshed as those of any
other. The Irish farmer will never forget Billie
Scully and Ballycooke, and the name of Scully is
never mentioned in an Irish farmstead without an
execration. The candidate selected to fight
Davitt, Healy, McCarthy, Sullivan and Sexton
is a very poor specimen of a man or a speaker,
and no influence could have induced the Kilkenny
farmers to be very enthusiastic in their support
of a Scully.— Kilkenny.
Victoria lias again lowered her inter-
colonial and foreign import and export returns for
the year, and is steadily concentrating her atten-
tion upon domestic self-support. Eor the year end-
ing 1890 the total Customs returns are £1,000,000
less than for the previous 12 months, and this ten-
dency towards decrease has occurred almost un-
interruptedly since 1874. The excess' of imports
oyer exports has increased by a couple of millions
since 1889. This, however, is attributable to the
abnormal purchases made by Victorians since the
late Exhibition of European and American
machinery, and in no wise represents a drifting
towards a suicidal Freetrade policy.
Stafford Brothers, Brundamba Colliery
(Q.), have had a row with the local miners’ union,
to which they have just issued this ultimatum
We have no further desire to arbitrate with you, since
it is hopeless to expect that anything like a permanent
conciliation can ever take place between a section of our
present workmen and ourselves. We shall shortly have
to part company from these. We shall endeavour to carry
on our mine in our own way, without seeking guidance
from you or appealing to arbitration, and if we are pre-
vented from so doing we propose just to sit quietly down
and do nothing.
This will be all very well provided the State
doesn’t place on all coal-mines a tax which will
make them too hot to hold unless in full work.
Once more they are going to keep green
the memory of “Convict Day.” In the name of
all that is Australian, why cannot they select
some more fitting day to celebrate the birth of
Australia as a white man’s country? Why do
they not select the April morning on which James
Cook first cast eyes on our shores ?—a worthy
man and a worthy event. Or even the January
day when Dampier first saw and cursed the
country ? Cook’s discovery had much more to do
with the colonisation of Australia than Phillip’s
settlement—and certainly is much more worthy
of celebration. However, some day Australia
will have a Fourth of July to orate on.
The 35-million French loan has been ten-
dered for many times ovei-, thus showing the
stability of the Republic. We are close on the
centenary of Mirabeau’s death, which loosened
Robespierre. The Revolution marches on, Robe-
spierre and Co. only affording the first volcanic
detonations of Vesuvius, The newest “Encyclo-
pedia Britannica ” will show you how, he, Danton,
and even Marat have been libelled. Why, don’t
you know that Fonseca, of Brazil, is a cannibal in
private, eating children ? The Republic has been
grounded on the rock ever since Louis XVI. was
guillotined. Monarchism, it is as plain as the
nose on the face of a six-bottle man, is dying out
—ebbing away.

Society
Sydney has got its New Governor, but has
not yet had time to make up its mind about him-
He is not handsome, but then, “handsome is a?
handsome does ” —he has plenty of money, and if
he only spends it in real earnest and manages to
get his donations advertised as Baron Carington
used to do, Australia will arise as one man and
call him blessed.
# * #
In appearance Lord Jersey is a regular
brick, especially about the hair. He is a “slum-
mocky ” potentate, but, then, he looks as if he
had a sense of humour. After the dapper beauty
of Baron Mount Rennie, Jersey’s appearance is
at first blush rather depressing, When we first
gazed upon him it occurred to us that the incar-
nation of the bacillus tuberculosis, finding Europe
too hot for it, had escaped to a Colonial Governor-
ship and £7OOO a year.
* « •
The new Governor’s public welcome was
comparatively cold. But that was in no small
degree owing to the fact that he came without his
wife, who, when she arrives, will get a reception
of her own. Lady Jersey is not so dainty and
doll-like as Lady Carington, but has a most
attractively intelligent face, which will go far to
redeem her lard’s abject failure as a professional
beauty.
# # #
In Lillie Langtry’s lovely face
The “ Jersey Lily ” has its place,
And strange to say the “Jersey rose”
Asserts itself in ——.
Every man who fills in the two words we have
left out may consider himself entitled to our best
blessing—an article which usually we do not dis-
pose of under two-and-six spot cash.
*, * #
The arrival of Governor Jersey may be
expected to bring quite a number of people into
fashion whose physical peculiarities have caused
them, hitherto, to be looked on with dislike by
the ladies and with suspicion by the members of
the temperance societies.
* # *
The question arises—will Lady Jersey,
when she comes, grant a social amnesty, or will
she adhere strictly to the lines laid down by Lady
Carington? The Bulletin doesn’t quite see why
the trouble should be kept up for ever.
# * #
Some wicked newspaper-man played off a
cruel joke upon Mr. Crick, M.L.A., who it was
alleged in print was among the M.P.’s who
attended at Government House, Sydney, to re-
ceive Governor Jersey. Whereupon Paddy wrote
the S.M. Herald explaining that he was not at
Government House to receive the Governor, nor
was he presented to His Excellency.
# # #
And what a cruel rascal was the caitjffwho
traded off this in the Society column of Sydney
D.T. :
A large number of ladies viewed the procession and
arrival of the Governor on Thursday, the points of van-
tage being the upper windows and balconies of the Lands
Office and Colonial Secretary’s buildings. Lady Parkes
and a party of friends were noticed as holding a good
position for a look-out on the arrival of-the vice-regal
party and the procession as it defiled through the gates
and down the tree-lined road to Government House.
At the foot of the big stairs in Government House
when the new Governor arrived, there sat about
a dozen ladies, including no less than eight mem-
bers of the inevitable Stephen family, Mrs. Carl
Fischer, and the wives of a couple of military
officers.
# # #
Undoubtedly the handsomest equipage at
Gov. Jersey’s levee was that of Mr. Carrie Swain*
# # #
In the welcome to Governor Jersey the
N.S.W. Primrose League was conspicuous by its
absence. Has ridicule killed the tribe of fops?
Or, is the League saving itself for the arrival of
Dame Jersey ?
# # #
The Adelaide Natives have decided to
substitute “Our Native Land” for the toast
“The land we live in” at all future meetings.
They have, however, decided to stick to the toast
of “The Queen,” and the yelling of the National
Anathema is as powerful as ever.
# * ■#
If The Bulletin had its way—which it
will one of these days—it would on taking
the chair at a public gathering, try to settle the
Royalty question at one fell swoop, thus: —
“ Gentlemen, we see around us at this board the
citizens of many nations over whom monarchs
rule and have ruled. We therefore ask you to
drink to the health of all living monarchs and to
the memory of all dead ones ; also to the healths
and memories of all their respective wives, hus-
bands, mistresses and children, legitimate and
illegitimate.” Such a toast would be fair and
comprehensive, and would give pleasure to all.
« * *
The cableman perpetrated a little joke on
Gov. Hopetoun a week or two ago. He cabled
that Lady Hopetoun had undergone a painful
operation, and was now improving, and also that
Lady Kintore was doing well in London. Hopey
was flabbergasted and, being a Scotchman, didn’t
see the joke. He wired to know what the
opei’ation was about, and learned to his relief
that not only had there been no operation per-
formed, but the idea had not even been
suggested. The good-natured cable-fiend (who
had all along only wanted to help the Goy. in
explaining the departure of her ladyship) didn’t
mind this rebuff in the least, but went on his way
smiling, and a week later calmly and unblushingly
wired that Lady Kintore had arrived in the old
country.
# * *
There is a great row over the opening day
of St. Paul’s Cathedral, Melbourne, being re-
stricted to subscribers of £25 apiece, and certain
it is that nobody out of the Evangelists, or Acts
of the Apostles, would have got in. The poor ye
have always with you—and they are a confounded
nuisance. We knew a journalist (not a clergy-
man) who said, “D the horny-handed !” The
clergy don’t say so—exactly.)
A FINE OLD CHESTNUT!
Mabel (meditatively) : “So our new Governor has red hair, ugh?”
MAMMA ( diplomatically): “Golden, my dear, golden!”
MABEL [demurely) : “Oh, of course, eighteen -carat to be sure, no less!”
Canon Barlow, of Toowoomba, who was
recently elected to the Bishopric of North Queens-
land, is" said to have been severely exercised in his
mind as to whether he would accept the billet or
not. The sphere of labour thus opened to him is
considerable, in fact, immense, and the Canon is
a modest man. The income is in inverse ratio to
the labour, and the Canon is a thrifty man. How-
ever, it is believed that the leggings and the title
carried the day.
* # ■< *
Roberts, of the Dunedin Exhibition, has
been offered a C.M.G.-ship, and thinks of de-
clining it, as he fully expected to be K.C.M.G. It
would be very regretable if Roberts should per-
sist in his unseemly determination. Consider the
feelings of her Most Gracious Majesty, Roberts,
and take it—it will help to meet butchers’ bills.
Start a brewery, and then if you don’t come out
on the XXX list, console yourself with the
reflection that the resurrection is not far ahead
and then stars will be a drug in the market.
# * *
The fashionable Mrs. Maybriek killed her
victim, her husband, from motives similar to
those which actuated the latest sensational
English murderess, Mrs. Pearcey—she wished
to rid herself of an obstacle to the complete grati-
fication of her unholy passion. But Mrs. May-
brick’s crime was far worse than that of Mrs.
Pearcey, for while the latter slew her victim right
away, possibly in a fit of ungovernable rage arising
out of some dispute which she may have had
with Mrs. Hogg (it may .be noted that
Pearcey was an unquestionable epileptic, the
history of attacks from which she had suffered
being recorded in the hospital book), Maybriek
calmly and stoutly and remorselessly administered
to her suffering spouse one dose of poison after
another day by day. Mrs. Maybriek, concerning
whose sanity there were no doubts, had powerful
social influence at her back, and was reprieved ;
Mrs. Pearcey, who was a nobody, lias been care-
fully hanged. It is such things as this which
make people wonder whether the scales of justice
aren’t loaded.
# * *
During the Pearcey-Hogg murder-trial the
Court was crowded day after day with “peopleof
family ” —mothers and daughters, husband's and
wives, brothers and sisters —armed with ppera-
glasses, sherry-flasks and sandwich-cases.
# # *
Dead dukes seem to have been a drug on
the market last week. Bedford, one of the
wealthiest and-most grasping of London land-
owners, has passed away to regions where the bad
language of his tenants can’t pursue him. The
old curmudgeon was probably the best-hated
autocrat in the London W. C. district, and it may
be taken for granted that many a bottle of
champagne was cracked in honour of the demise
of the man who made each “Bedford lease an
incitement to revolution. In the art of raising l
rents and imposing “conditions” upon the
denizens of his estate the Duke had few rivals
even in the House of Peers. Out of pure
cussedness he refused to entertain any scheme for
the improvement of Covent Garden Market,
whilst his ancestral fondness for blocking up
thoroughfares with iron gates amounted to a
disease. He belonged to the good old Tory school
this Bedford, and the fact that he pegged out
peacefully instead of being prematurely groaned
to death by an indignant populace is mainly
attributable to the care with which he played dark
off the weather bow, waiting for the Wrath that
has at last arrived.
# * *
The Duke of Somerset’s death, reported
by cable last week, gave a shock of surprise to the
toiling millions who were not previously aware
that the octogenarian had ever been born.
Although very blue-blooded, Somerset was not
what is known as a “great” duke. Anthony
Trollope was fond of satirising, in his innocuous
novels, the relative importance of such people,
and always quoted that fictitious old sinner, his
Grace of Omnium, as the typical “great’’duke.
Omnium took no part in politics, and had never
publicly distinguished himself in any direction.
But he enjoyed a private reputation for being
shockingly giddy in a senile, ducal kind of way,
and made it the business of his life not to “un-
bend,” wherefore sassiety, standing aside as
Omnium tottered along, accepted his greatness as
an article of faith. Somerset was simply a dull,
decent, vegetable sort of Duke who made no
difference. He didn’t even have one relative con-
cerned in the Cleveland-street scandal, Lord
Arthur Somerset being the son of Beaufort, a fact
which gives the latter a fair claim to be considered
a great duke, quite apart from his celebrated
friendship for Connie Gilchrist.
* # *
The association (more or less remote) of
Governors Carington, Jersey and Hopetoim with
the private banking trade “at home,” reminds
us that joint-stock banks have been knocking the
stuffing out of private county-family businesses
for years past, so it is about time that one or two
of those solemn venerable institutions went bung.
They don’t invite vulgar outside custom. If an
unknown person, suffering . from the name of
Jones, falters in, with the intention of opening
an account, he is asked for references and glared
at through gold-rimmed eyeglasses until he gives
up all idea of banking with the aristocracy. And,
seeing that it is so difficult to get one’s money
into these high-bred shops, the day cannot be far
off when it will be dashed hard to get the sugar
out again. This may be a vewwy dweadful para-
graph, but it is compiled on sound commercial
principles.
# * #
The Czar, it is cabled, has simply returned
the petition sent by the Lord Mayor of London
asking that the Russian Jews be persecuted no
more. However, in the long run the Jews will be
found capable of effectually taking their own part.
Thei-e seems to be little doubt that the difficulties
of Baring Brothers and Co., which have caused
such a sensation throughout the world, have been
immediately caused by the Jews. They saw their
opportunity and seized it in order to deal a blow
at the one great English house remaining in the
foreign banking business, and through it at the
Russian Government.
* * #
The N.S.W. Ca\ airy forces -ire to have
not only a full-dress uniform but a mounted band,
the musical instruments for which have already
been imported. These pieces of gorgeous extrava-
gance must, of course, have been personally
assented to by that fine showy old agent of the
Imperial Government, Sir Henry Parkes,
G.C.M.G., &c., &c. It is interesting to know
that “with the exception of the drummer, who is
to have a piebald, the bandsmen will bo mounted
on greys. The band will be the first of its kind
established in the colonies, and will, in all pro-
bability, make its appearance in the metropolis on
such occasions as the opening of Parliament and
the Queen’s Birthday Review.” In other words,
it is to ba used as a thread of “ the silken bonds,”
This absurd outlay is in strict keeping with the
grotesque vulgarity of the appearance of the
Sydney Lancers. Let us hope that the band will
be in full blast on the day of the opening of
Parkes’ Deadhouse.
* * *
By the way, we heard the other day a
story that casts a bright light upon the vain-
gloriousness of Parkes’ real character. A firm of
publishers had (probably with a view to a Govern-
ment subvention) got him to accept the dedica-
tion of a certain Australian book. The words of
the dedication, wherein was included a list of his
titles and gewgaws up to date, were submitted to
him in a proof, of which he approved. A day or
two afterwards he got word that the King of
Italy, in recognition of the consideration with
which the N.S.W. Government had received the
survivors of the ill-fated expedition of the Marquis
de Rays, had conferred on him a commandership
of the Order of the Dead Bud, or something of
the sort (we have a bad memory for kingly dis-
tinctions), and old man Parkes sate him down and
wrote to the publishers ; “You will have observed
that His Majesty the King of Italy has been
pleased to confer upon me the knightly Order of
the Dead Bud. Please have the dedication altered
in terms of the announcement.”
# # *
John Woods’ suggestion that the whole of
the unoccupied land in front of Sydney G.P.O.
should be converted into a square is a good idea.
But when Mr. Woods further artfully proposes
that the square “ should be named after some one
of our living politicians ” {i.e., Parkes) he deals in
absurdity. “Parkes’ Square!” Parkes never
was square.
* * *
Each morning two men may be observed
going the rounds of the flower-beds in Hyde Park,
Sydney, culling the choicest blossoms. These are
made into a bouquet and placed upon the break-
fast-table of a certain trustee. Common John
Smith is liable to the “cat” for touching these
same flowers. The lash is designed only for the
back of the man in workman’s garb.

# « *
The Agricultural Society of N.S. Wales
has, by its own resolution, added the word
“ Royal ”to its title. The action, says a Sydney
daily, is ultra vires, inasmuch as only the Queen
can confer the title. From this The Bulletin
infers that Queen Victoria has conferred the
title “ Royal ” on 2900 Australian public-houses.
* * •
Sydney Quarterly Magazine speaks in a
regretful but somewhat self-congratulatory tone
of the demise of the Centennial Magazine, The
Quarterly need not forget that the other mag.
paid, as a rule, for its literary matter, while its
own columns are filled by theglad-to-see-myself-in-
print effusions of subscribers, and that, despite a
brief lease of life, the Centennial leaves behind
an issue nearly as numerous as the Quarterly
can boast of in a career of seven years.
# * *
If any man of learning can solve this
scientific problem he will do a good turn to the
community. When a citizen who has been
liquoring profusely resolves to start for home, and
finds a difficulty in getting out of the pub. owing
to there being three doors in a row where there was
only one before, ought he to rush at the middle
door, or should he try the one at the furthest end?
Also, supposing he gets outside and sees two cabs
where there is really only one, is it the first one
that is there or the second, or is the real one in
between the two? Many respectable gentlemen
who get home with difficulty would be glad of
information on these points.
* * *
The Chinese-cook question again :
Dear Bulletin, —I have seen much of Chinese cooks—
in this land and in others. My aversion to their ways is
not likely to be less keen than that of any other of your
readers, seeing that 1 am “ bonnet blue ” myself, and
have the tradesman’s prejudice against cheap competi-
tion. Certainly their modes of work are dirty. Certainly,
also, the yarn which your “ Schduffin ’’-contributor sent
you may be true. At any rate it is does not involve
any impracticability, and its hideous possibility
was the more interesting to me, from my knowledge that
the sort of people who employ a Chinese cook are not
usually contented with the amount of work he can
reasonably do with his hands alone. But when you let
some “ new 7 chum” fellow, wiio itches with the desire to
“ go one better” than t’other, unnecessarily horrify people
with the iced cake blague (I don’t know an equivalent
word), and you then afterwards take from Lithgow
Lawson the confirmation, strong as Holy Writ, that he
has seen it done himself, it is time, in the interests
of truth and pure morality, to put a pound
cr two on the valve. The thing is not impossible. Few
things are impossible, they say. This is only impractic-
able, which is a far more difficult bill to fill. Tell you
what I did see once. I came into my kitchen late one
night—’twas in a big hotel up north, not so many years
back, and found my Chow third cook, “ mid codings on,”
paring his toe-nails with my pet “ couteau d’office.” He
left!— “Gros Bonnet.”
* # #
One of the Sydney Sunday papers modestly
calls itself “The People’s Watchdog.” Its hated
rival hereupon remarks that the watchdog afore-
said has got the mange.
* * *
The captain of the ship Peter Stuart,
whose chief-officer lately fatally shot at sea an
aggressive seaman named Curry, deposed at the
inquest that “he made an official entry in the
log-book shortly after the occurrence, but did not
read it to Curry, as he was unconscious and
suffering great pain.”
* * #
The following spectacle is one that may be
seen any day on a Sydney tram-or on any other
tram, for that matter. First of all an innocent
young female comes in and sits down with a
modest smile as if there were something amusing
in the mere fact of her presence in that particular
spot. Then she settles her dress, and puts herself
to rights generally, and smiles a great glad smile
that suggests the reflection of the morning sun on
the bright glass-eye of day. By-and-bye the con-
di*-*or asks for her fare, and she finds it in the
nethermost bowels of her pocket, and as she fishes
it out from that receptacle she smiles some more
with a look of Christian resignation as if she were
resolved to bear up against all manner of affliction,
and when he hands over the change her face lights
up with a smile of joyous intelligence as if she
had been staggered by a great inspiration. After
a while she smiles over again because she finds she
has to get out at the next corner, and when the
tram stops she smiles because it has stopped, and
as she gets out she trips on her dress and screams
in an engaging sort of way, and then she smiles
on general principles, and when the tram starts
she smiles for no reason in particular, and finally
she trots away still smiling. And meanwhile the
other female passengers have their souls filled with
murder, and long to heave her into the street on
account of her smile. Somehow she aggravates
them.
* * *
The amalgamation of the legal professions
received a practical confirmation in a recent
appointment. The N.S.W. Crown Solicitor’s
Department, having charge of all the cases before
the Land Board, has, it is said, entered into a
contract with a barrister practising at the N.S.W.
Bar that he shall accept a lump-sum per annum
in lieu of the fees which would be paid to him in
the ordinary course of professional work. The
alleged contract, being regarded as a violation of
barristerial etiquette, has caused quite a fuss b’
Wigland. v
# * #
The latest embezzlement case in Austra-
lia’s ho’y city is one against a local preacher, who
has a deficiency in his cash of £lOO or more, and
is missing. The religious one under attention
vanished in his official coat and belltopper when
the official came in to audit his cash. Keeping up
the average rate for the last five or ten years, the
Adelaide Stockade will soon be crammed chock-a-
block with unsuccessful saints and members of
the Y.M.C.A. Meanwhile, the young man who
drinks beer with his lunch and doesn’t ostenta-
tiously go to church seems happy, and his cash is
aggravatingly correct.

The headway made by cremation in Eng-
land is shown by the cremation, at Woking, of
the two most distinguished men who have died of
late, Kinglake, the historian, and Baron Huddle-
ston.

The hero of a certain abduction-case, now
incarcerated in Darlinghurst, has gained 211 b. in
weight inside two months. Love is a great fat-
reducer.

monstrous 20-foot gold chain which he wears
round his neck and—beg pardon, Mr. M‘Elhone
his “ binjie.” The chain was given him, he says,
by his brethren of the lowa hotelkeepers’ associa-
tion, and on each link is stamped the name of a
member of the fraternity. lowa being a prohi-
bition state, the publicans can’t sell alcohol unless
they break the law, and so it may happen that
each linkof theßallingallian chain doesn’t represent
several hundred damned souls as would probably
be the case were such a chain constructed under
the auspices of any Australian society of whisky-
slingers. The gallant Colonel avers that he is in
favour of State inebriate asylums, which, as he is
also in favour of public-houses, must be regarded
as a most fortunate thing. He asserts that the
American Democrats are Freetraders. _ “ The
wave of progressive thought,” says he, “is carry-
ing ns all towards the bourne of Freetrade,”
(though when he talked to us he used good, prosaic
public-house phraseology—this poetic sentence is
ascribed to him by a Sydney Daily Telegraph
reporter). When he turned up at The Bulletin
office we asked him whether the American Demo-
crats really were Freetraders. He said “Yes.”
Then said we: “Oh, you are all Georgeites?”
“ Oh, no !” replied the Colonel, indignantly and
obviously on the verge of a violent passion—
“ George is nobody. tie has no following to
speak of.” “ But,” said we, “ George is the only
real Freetrader. He is the only politician who
believes in allowing all foreign and nigger-labour
products to come in free.” “Oh,” answered the
Colonel, “ we don’t believe in unnecessarily taxing
things which we can’t produce to advantage.”
“Well, then,” we replied, “you are a discrimi-
nating Protectionist.” “No,” replied Colonel
Ballingall, hideou b lisgusted with our beastly
ignorance in conn aig him with such a mean
and impotent institution as Henry George, we
believe in a revenue-tariff which will incidentally
assist our American manufactures.” Moral:
There arc no real Freetraders in the whole world,
except the cranks with the wild eyes and the long
hair.

The largo, fat female who conducts visitors
round one of Hingland’s ancestral halls is said to
reel-off her everlasting yarn in this fashion:
“ Here we ’ave the small dining-’all in which the
Dook was assashinated in 1529. To the right is
the spot where he received the first thrust from
the dadger ; to the left is where he fell martially
wounded ; in front is the hearth marked with a
cross in black tiles where ’is lofty spirit took its
flight; and adjoining the white marble clnmminey-
piece is a secret closet where our rupper ouse-
maids keeps their brooms an’ buckets; an
anything you like to give me will be received with
thanks.”
# * *
“R.C.D.,” who writes to the daily press,
is quite right. The quack-medicine man who
exposes in the shop-window of a leading Sydney
street, a hideous picture illustrative of disease,
should be proceeded against by the police. Ihe
Bulletin presumes that if the quack exposed in
the window the diseased man instead of the
picture of the diseased man, the exhibition would
be officially regarded as obscene. _lf the original
would be obscene the photograph is obscene like-
wise. To show photographs of ghastly cankers in
order to frighten people into buying nostrums is
as offensive as showing sores in public to excite
nitv —a custom long since put down by law.

From a recent decision of Melbourne City
Court it would appear that accredited parsons
who inadvertently annex books from the local
Public Library share the kleptomaniacal privi-
leges accorded to perfect ladies the scene of whose
operations is the fashionable draper’s shop.
* * *
There is a fine, full-flavoured snuffle about
the following extract from a letter written by one
of the band of holy young men and women who,
last month, sailed from Sydney to join the Inland
China mission ;
It is such a privilege to be travelling with Mr. Taylor ;
he is a real spiritual father to us all. The order of the
day is to rise soon after 5 a.m., private Bible-reading till
breakfast, then a lesson in the Chinese language, and
after that united Bible-reading until dinner, then Chinese
again for several hours, interspersed with talking and
hearing lessons. After tea, if not too hot, we write and
sing for a while, and after another time for prayer and
Scripture-reading together, we retire for the night, to
sleep if possible.
Just notice, dear friends, that all spiritual work
is done “ together ” by these good young men and
women ! Anyone who has ever travelled on a ship
and observed the ways of men and maids with
regard to ocean choir-practice, will fully appre-
ciate the humour and the probabilities of the
situation.
* # #
Somebody wants to know the Index Ex-
purgatorius principle of Melbourne Public
Library. Hawthorne’s works are expurgated of
“The Scarlet Letter,” and nobody must read the
volumes of Bentley’s Miscellany which contain
“Jack Sheppard;” but the dirty old English
drama is furnished galore, for the thumbs of
Loaferdom. A reader inquired lately for “Tom
Jones,” but the librarian said it was “worn out,”
and almost hinted that the request was not
respectable.
* * #
Not very long ago Melbourne was favoured
with a decent sort of pressman, fresh from
London, and loaded to the muzzle with Debrett
and Garter King-at-Arms, with Lord Nozoo and
the Ladies Nozoo on the brain. He got shut up
by Democritus, who burst out one day, in the
midst of all the fellows, while Gommy was holding
forth :—“Look, you’re the most dangerous revo-
lutionist who ever came here, with all your yarns
,about the filthy, beastly ways of your beloved
aristocracy.” In fact, he did remind one of the
London social (evil) correspondent of the Mel-
bourne Argus.
# # #
“ The past, present, and future truthfully
reviled ” is the advertisement of a local prophetess
in the palmistry business. There seems to be a
lot of probability about the statement, too, when
you look at it.
# * *
A young Englishwoman, only four months
married, has committed suicide because she didn’t
know how to keep house properly. She left a
letter asking her husband to marry some other
girl who will know how many beans make five.
How different to the young Australian matron,
who tells Adolphus to cook the dinner himself,
and then hits him across the head with the baby,
if he mildly suggests that steak is all the better
for being “ done through.” Fancy one of “our
girls ” killing herself because there was no
mustard on the premises !
* # *
The other morning at 3 a.m. a raid was
made upon the house of a resident of Woodville-
road, Granville, Sydney, by four revolver-armed
policemen and an inspector, who carried a warrant
to arrest the resident for running a “ private still.”
All the members of the family were much sur-
prised at the charge; but the stern officers of
justice were very confident about the matter,
and, saying they “had already inspected the
still,” would insist on dragging their prisoner in
his night-garments to the end of his garden or
paddock (to convince him that they knew their
business and had not gone to the trouble of getting
a warrant for nothing)—only to discover that
the suspected “still” was a huge incubator at
work hatching eggs ! The incident, however, has
a serious side to it, as it considerably added to the
serious illness of the suspected man’s wife.
* * *
The Bulletin presumes that the police
consider themselves safe in the abovementioned
matter, on the good old well-worn ground that
they had a warrant and “ reasonable and probable
cause.” If, however, Government chooses to keep
in its employ a set of officials so stupid and
uninstructed that they confound a machine for
chicken-hatching with a contrivance for cheating
the revenue somebody ought surely to pay a
penalty.

Jack Hustle: “Will you marry me?”
Rita Rustle : “This is so sudden - give me time.”
Jack Hustle: “You can’t afford to waste any
more time. You must be 20 now. Say yes,
Rita.”— Puck.

Great interest was excited at Wagga on
Sunday week through the public immersion in the
Murrumbidgee of a young woman who had been
received into some fold or other. This is the sort
of religion which satisfies the spiritual cravings of
the man of the City of Crows. They have no
ballet to comfort them in Wagga.

Heredity of Crime.
CURIOUS PHASES AND THE DIREFUL
RESULTS OF INHERITED DISEASE.
Doubtless crime is committed from the mere
love of it. The identity of “Jack the Ripper”
remains undiscovered, and so we do not know
what impelled him to the commission of his
atrocious crimes. Little doubt he was insane, his
actions controlled by an abnormal condition of
the mind ; it is plain none of the ordinary reasons
for crime prevailed in this case. He was, perhaps,
a victim of some hereditary taint, which drove
him to acts against which his will revolted, and
from which in his rational moments his soul
recoiled in horror. A French novelist of realism
makes the hero of a late novel of such a character.
He was a locomotive engineer, a thoroughly com-
petent and reliable man ; but if he chanced to be-
hold the white throat of a woman, a wild frenzy
seized him ; he could not control a desire to
plunge a knife in it. He was aware of his in-
firmity, fought it with stubborn will, denied him-
self the society of women, and for years controlled
himself. At last he meets his fate ; finds pleasure
in her society, with no return of his old longing
for blood, until he flatters himself he is safe. But
one night she meets him with gleaming throat
bare ; the uncontrollable impulse seizes him ; he
cannot resist ; as she throws her loving arms
about him, he thrusts the knife into her neck,
and she falls, a victim to the madness of tainted
blood. Fortunately for the victims of ordinary
forms of inherited disease they may protect them-
selves before it obtains dominance in the system.
Inherited kidney disease is especially dangerous,
but may be successfully resisted. “In 1883 1
was so near death’s door with an inherited and
complicated case of Bright’s disease,” writes James
H. Dove, 217 Bald win-street, Alpena, Mich.,
August 11, 1890. “which had baffled all remedies
and medical assistance obtainable, that I was
given up by friends and physicians as beyond all
recovery. In this extremity I was induced to try
Warner’s safe cure, which very soon gave me
relief, and led me to persevere in its use, until I
again became able to attend to my business. My
case is well known in this community, and I feel
fully warranted in recommending Warner’s safe
cure in cases of kidney disease, knowing full well
that I would have long since been in my grave
had it not been for the use of this remedy.” Men
and women inherit genius, talent, all mental and
physical qualities ; and it- is now well established
that all phases of physical as well as mental disease
are transmissible.


Wolfe’s Schnapps for lowness- of spirits.
Wolfe’s Schnapps should be in every house.
An exhilarating cordial is Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Wolfe’s Schnapps—for the liver and kidneys.

WORD COMPETITION.
STOCKTON SCHOOL Op ARTS.
the Committee of the above Institution offers a prize
of £lO 10s. to the competitor who can make the most
words, under conditions hereunder, from the word
“ Municipalities.” Asa-second prize, for the second
highest number of words, a trophy will be given. No
word will be accepted that cannot be found in either the
Imperial or Chambers Etymological Dictionary. Provided,
however, that all plurals of such words may be given as
separate words. No word must he used more than once.
The fact that any word might be made more than once by
using duplicate letters contained in the competitive word
shall not affect this last point. No word will be allowed
that contains any letter oftener than the same occurs in
the competitive word. Competitors should arrange then
words alphabetically. No member of the Committee will
tie allowed to compete. All lists must be sent in, addressed
to the undersigned, on or before 25th March next. Every
list must be accompanied by a fee of 2s. 6d. Any com-
petitor wishing to have his list returned, must send
stamped addressed envelope for the purpose. The result
will be published in the issue of this paper of 4th April
next.
H. A. GRAHAM, Secretary.

One day recently a large Australian retail
and importing firm advertised for a book-keeper,
offering the munificent stipend of 25s. a week and
his daily dinner.
Two immensely rich rajahs advertise in
the Indian papers for European wives. Photos,
have to be forwarded, and it is implied that, in
the words of the song, she is to be the only one.
But Bluebeard is tioklesome.
Truth (Sydney) has the audacity to
mangle one of Bnmton Stephens’ poems the
“Ode to a Black Gin” —and to publish it as
original, under a new nom-de-plume, “Tea and
Coffee !”
The chief engineer to the Melbourne
tramway job has patented an invention which
will, he says, reduce the cast of laying cable tram-
lines by £7000 pet mile. More grease for that
fatted pig, the original shareholder.
As the N.S.W. District Courts Act allows
retiring D.C. judges but half salary as a pension,
it would be impossible for one of the two sug-
gested retirements to take place, for the simple
reason that as the judge cannot live on his salary
he could not possibly exist on his pension.

bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. Tn these days of extortion, the
above institution should about lib a long-felt
want.*

The Referee.
“Fancy a single mare being thamother of three
winners of different ages at the one meeting and on the
one day !” says a Sydney Sunday paper. Such shameful
depravity on the part of a single mare should be inquired
into by the Social Purity Brigade at once; And this
reminds us of the true story of the Western man who
returned a £2O Maiden Plate money to the secretary of a
racing club near Bathurst, with the explanatiojjthaj; he
had discovered his mare was in foal.at the time 'Sjfle, won,
and consequently could not fairly be entitled to 'such a
prize. He was a twin brother to the good young man
that died.
» # *,
Australian gentlemen amateur jocks., it is
Understood, ride for glory only. One of the boldest and
best of them recently piloted a winner,for the Squire of
the East and he was rewarded thus :—“ Charlie, come in
End have a look at the Cup you wort for me.” Fact.
# * *
Cricketer Ferris is going to England for a long
Stay. The game is going to the dogs in Australia.
* * «
A detective is now regularly employed at
N.S.W. race-meetings keeping an eye on and ejecting
Welshers and Spielers.
# # #
A N.S.W. doctor, for shooting game in the
Close season and a publican for cooking it, were fined
heavily, the other day. Which reminds us that the
Police Magistrate at Forbes, N.S.W. is an ardent sports-
man and a staunch upholder of the law. There are other
ardent sportsmen in Forbes who are not such staunch
supporters of the law, and these did a little duck-shooting
in the close season not many years ago. The proceeds
Were duly handed over to a certain ancient hotel-
keeper with instructions to “do them proper” for lunch.
They were done “proper,” but as ill-luck would have it,
the P;M. came to lunch on that day of all days in the
year, and sat an unseemly time over his meal. The
illegal “ shootists ’" had their ducks spoilt, and have been
blessing the P.M. ever since.
* * *
The Australian lady-cricketers are getting dis-
organised already, and threaten to break up. A Sydney
showman tried to hire the team for public exhibition at
20s. per girl for each day’s play, but the captainess
rejected the offer rather peremptorily, partly because she
considered the terms inadequate, and partly because she
didn’t desire that any miserable male speculator should
have a lien over her. When the team heard of it, how-
ever, and realised that they had missed a chance of forty
ice-creams per diem, they fired much strong language at
the bosj of the show and demanded her resignation
only, as it happened, she didn’t resign. She had started
the thing, and supplied the brains and the experience,
and most of the energy, and a good part of the money ;
and if the whole club resigns she will stay on just the
same, and play by herself. And now the rest of the
cricketers don’t know whether to leave, or to hit her on
the head with a stump, or simply to sulk and cast reflec-
tions on her figure and her hair, and utter unjust
aspersions concerning her feet and her bonnet, and pass a
resolution that she is horrid. So there !
# * *
The totalisator is very popular in W.A. At the
recent meeting of the W.A. Turf Club at Perth, during
the two days no less than £5OOO passed through the
machine. There was little or no open betting, as the few
local books were unable to give anything like reasonable
odds. Last year an Adelaide bookmaker came across,
but he was only moderately well supported, and did not
repeat his visit this year.
# * #
The lamented Moorhouse, who was debarred
from swallowing “ great masses of food ” some time ago,
makes the most original suggestion which we have yet
heard of for the abolition of gambling. He proposes that
all gamblers should agree not to take a stake when they
win, but to pay it all over to some charitable institution,
and by this means he considers that, nap, 100, horse-
racing and similar pursuits would be freed from that
element of unholy greed which tends to demoralise their
votaries. There is a Christian element hitched on some-
where to this simple scheme, but The Bulletin is over-
come by emotion, and backs out silently without any
further comment. A bishop like that is altogether too
harmless for his job.
# * *
Mr. Billy Murphy, who first made his name in
Sydney and always got the best of fair treatment there,
can’t forget the licking he got from Griffo, and blames
everybody and everything but the “flashness” that pre-
vented him from training for the result. “Mr.” Griffo,
the champion, should profit by the example of his
defeated rival, hilt he probably won’t.
* # #
The Adelaide people are mad over Giffen’s
great score against Victoria, and an effort is being made
to raise £lOO by shilling subscriptions for the ohampion.
The leading men in the matter are Sir E. T. Smith, who
has yelled for “ George ” for years, and the portly Caleb
Peacock. The latter went to England on the same boat as
the first Australian team. Apropos, “Tommy” Horan
formed a minstrel company on board-ship. “Tommy”
was “ bones,” and Peacock acted with success as “ inter-
locutor.”

of Gold and Sheen there’s nothing in England to stop
Carbine winning the distance weight-for-age-races.
# * *
Peall is 36 years old, he began playing billiards
at 12. He has publicly made over 2000 in a break at least
six times, once scored' 222 off the red ball alone, spot-
barred, and lias put a thousand together on one occasion
in forty minutes.
# * *
According to general expectation, John
Dempsey, the gallant victor of 50 fights, for years the un-
disputed middle-weight champion of the world, was
beaten by Robert Fitzsimmons, the Scotch-Maoriiand
blacksmith, inside 13 rounds after a hurricane fight for
£2200. La Blanche heat Dempsey some months ago.
Few fighters have the brains of Larry Foley, who knew
when to give the game up.

luid NViinjiuis, 2|yds. The only one of the lot who would

of Malone or Samuels, rpid thought that either to win at

Malone hadjiot improved on his amateur form in the old
country, '

* * #
Ike Weir boxed four rounds with Abe Willis at
the Australian Athletic Club, Sydney, on Saturday even-
ing last. Ike—he comes from Belfast, Ireland—was
attired in green trunks and red stockings (the green above
the red), and looked like a song-and-dance merchant.
However, he stripped splendidly, and is one of the
smartest men with head, hand and foot, we’ve seen any-
where, but his staying powers are doubtful. Weir never
attempted to box, he leaped about like a kangaroo,
ducked under Willis’ arms, hit round his back, contorted his
face and wriggled his body till everyone roared, except
Abe, who got very wild. They were to have boxed six
rounds, but in the fourth Weir went queer inside, and
simply walked round Willis crab-fashion till the latter
worthy, disgusted and annoyed, threw a handspring,
turned a somersault, and finished the farce. Whereupon
Larry Foley remarked; “If Weir’s wise lie won’t meet
Fuller on Monday,” and he didn’t. Then Oriffo, having
got leave from the other club to postpone his match
with Powell, if necessary, mounted tiro platfoim,
and said he wouldn’t let Weir go away saying
no one in Australia would fight him, and Foley
offered to back Griffo for any sum from £5OO to
£lO,OOO, and allow Weir from £lOO to £3OO as expenses,
according to stake. Then Weir said engagements, pre-
vented him staying here, and promised Griffo a big purse
and a good time in ’Frisco if he’d fight there. Griffo
then made six more speeches, and nothing was done.
Mr. Ike “ Spider” Weir—“ Flea” would have been a more
appropriate nickname—is a very clever young man and a
decent sort of chap, but he sings comic songs with too
much abandon, plays the piano too well, smokes too many
cigars, and shifts lemonade too handily to last out a hardy
youngster in a severe battle.
* # *
Says “ Pendragon ” (and he .ought to know)
“ I have got it into my head that more men have been
killed with the gloves than with the naked fist; that all
the thick ears and flattened noses of fighting men have
been given with the gloves, and not with the naked fist :
that, though I have seen a good many prize-fights, I
never saw- a man knocked out with the naked fist, while 1
have known scores who have been knocked out with the
gloves ; and that only a couple of years ago, at a ‘ car-
nival’ of the Boxing Association, whose president swore
to the mildness, tenderness, and innocnonsncss of boxing,
I saw more .punishment given with full-sized gloves (quite
lOoz. gloves) in three minutes than I had ever before seen
given with a naked fist in double the time. ’

What an uiglonpus ending hath the sporting
career of many men and beasts ! Parole, winner of the
Oity and Suburban Handicap in 1879, the Chester Cup,
and several other big English races, was taken back to
America, where everyone thought his owner would treat
him well. He recently ran last in a race for hacks and
hunters, when two prairie ponies gave him weight, and
was entered as “ Parole, pedigree unknown.”
