Premier Munro, in his capacity as a
Scotchman, is evidently resolved that bang does
not go saxpence with any unnecessary velocity.
When it was proposed to celebrate the end of the
session with the customary £(>00 picnic down the
harbour, under the thin device of inspecting the
whitewashed fortifications and looking with an
unintelligent eye at the gun which won’t go off,
the old man nobly responded that the members
would have to stand the expense among them ;
and he and his colleagues even paid their own
railway fares when they went down to orate at
Geelong. Until his majority is a little better
organised Munro evidently intends to manifest a
lofty contempt for coin and all other worldly con-
siderations.
There is a lot of unconscious humour
about, the Australian Natives’Association. Here
is a sample:—
Among the correspondence dealt with were letters from
several branches, asking the permission of the board to
congratulate Mr. Peacock, M.L.A., on his inclusion in the
Munro Ministry. It was decided to inform the branches
in question that in the opinion of the board it was quite
unnecessary to congratulate any of the members of the
A.N.A. on attaining public positions or other honourable
distinctions, as the time had passed when the attainment
of such positions or'distinctions by Australian Natives
could be regarded as anything unusual.
Poor Peacock with his appalling laugh is only an
honorary Minister, without portfolio or screw or
any recognised situation whatever. And now one
half of the A.N.A. wants to congratulate him on
the surprising circumstance that he should be
allowed to hang on gratis at the tail of a Cabinet
in his own country, while all the power and the
money is taken by imported politicians ; and the
other half points out elaborately that the time is
past when anyone need be surprised to see a Native
rise to eminence, and that though the spectacle of
Peacock running in the mud behind the Ministerial
vehicle, and trying to hang on, is a noble and
cheering thing, still it must be accepted with
dignified calm as a matter of everyday occurence.
And now the toughest point of etiquette in exist-
ence is badly in want of settlement, for Peacock
has got to decide whether he should thank the
A.N.A. with all his heart for its obvious good in-
tentions, or whether he should cowhide that body
for its infamous inability to express itself, and
then sue it for libel.
Fortune for a few pounds.—Buy land in the
Capital City of West Australia. W. J. Douglas,
96 Bathurst-street, Sydney.

Wolfe's Schnapps—It is grand.
An obviously - inspired letter signed
“ Lever,” concerning the N.S.W. railway depart-
ment troubles, has appeared in the Sydney daily
pap rs. The Bulletin says obviously-inspired
because although it was printed in the S.M.
Herald on the day after it was written, it never-
theless found a place in the Sydney Daily Tele-
graph in leaded type 24 hours later still. Here is
a slab of it:—
The Railway Commissioners have been appointed for
seven years, and during that period clause 15 of the bill
bringing them into existence vests the absolute right of
the removal of any member of the staff in their hands.
Let the public rest assured that the Commissioners haye
not acted in tins matter from caprice, hut deliberately so,
from causes which to them are sufficient. It would
obviously he as absurd to demand their reasons in this as
in any other case of dismissal which has occurred, as it
would he an infringement of their undoubtedly vested
tights. They are lenient enough in their treatment of
their employes, and would only exercise their
right of dismissal under a painful sense of duty.
The Commissioners may rest assured that their
~ 1
Mil
• jp IF * 1 Pi**, m
...I
mmm
eight 4- r-V Length 4’-TlO-, Depth 2'-. t-.
authority in this and in any ulterior measures which they
may be forced to take will be upheld by the vast majority
in this country, who place confidence in their wisdom,
founded on their two years’ beneficent control of the
railways ; and that those who would seek to discredit
either their discretion or ability are in a hopeless, although
a blatant, minority'.
“Absurd to give reasons, indeed !” It is just as
“ absurd ” to give to the public reasons why a man
should be hanged or imprisoned as to give reasons
for depriving him of his situation and living. No
better example of the insolence and irresponsi-
bility of the bureaucracy which in a dozen quar-
ters menaces public freedom in Australia has been
afforded than “ Lever’s ” letter. The public are
going to have reasons, inasmuch as they are the
employers and the masters of both the Commis-
sioners and the men, and the final judges between
them. Who are the Railway Commissioners that
they should expect to burke a criticism which
beats fiercely on the Ministers of the Crown and
on the Judges of the Supreme Court ?

Two cables ;—
No. 1. Complaint having been made to Sir Francis
Dewington, Governor of the Imperial British East Africa
Co.’s territory, that the Company’s agent at Witu had
behaved in a manner which was considered insulting to
the German flag, Sir Francis at once tendered an apology,
and removed the offending agent from his position. The
apology was accepted by the German representatives.
No. 2. Lord Salisbury distinctly refuses to make any
concessions to the Portuguese, beyond those embraced in
the agreement drawn up by the representatives of the
two countries.

Isaac O’Neil Weir Esq., bantam-weight
pug, arrived in Sydney on Saturday by the
A'ameda. His first go will be with Griffo.
When Murphy and Griffo fought, Murphy gave
in for want of condition, only, he could have gone
on all night so far as punishment was concerned,
and both in their best “nick,” the writer would
back Murphy to win. Now, whilst his condition
lasted, Weir made a perfect fool of Murphy,
hitting him when and where he liked, and to give
the Maorilander his due, it was only his dogged
courage which pulled him through to the point
when Weir fell weak. On these lines Griffo must
lay himself out to take some sort of a licking
before he wears Weir down, if it is his fate so
to do.
Advise your friends to drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.

Stage Versicles.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
THE THEATRICAL MANAGERS’ PLAINT.
Suggested by a song in “ The Gondoliers,”
In days of old when shows were few
And “ settings-up ” not cost-ly,
The runners yearly richer grew—
At least they prospered most-ly ;
But now, when shows are all around,
And each is bound to hold its ground
By adding to expen-ses,
Though prices stick about the same,
Why, players of the cut-throat game
(Who’ve only got themselves to blame)
Can scarce retain their sen-ses.
’Tis sad, although ’tis true, you know,
In countries young and sun-ny,
When folks can’t go to every show
Whose class is high and prices low,
Then bitter tears from bosses flow,
For none are making mon-ey.
Chorus:
Though you and we care not a D.,
But laugh Ha ! Ha ! in joyous glee,
And think it rather fun-ny,
There’s not much joke in going broke
For want of making mon-ey.
Two hundred legs upon the stage,
Or casts “unpreceden-ted,”
Are wanted in the present age
Ere patrons feel contented ;
Five shillingsworth for half-a-crown
They need to bring them into town
Anon they’ll ask for train-fare;
And buyers taught to pick and choose
Are always likely to refuse
The best of goods, so showmen Jose—
They cannot long the strain bear.
Of costs that grow and prices low
G. Musgrove is a darn-er :
When biz is slow at every show
Great Rignold smites his brow a blow,
And Messrs. Brough and Boucico
Hate Williamson and Gar-ner.
Chorus :
Though matters thus don’t trouble us,
They say that Rignold makes a fuss,
And Musgrove is a darn-er,
And roads are rough for Bouce. and Brough,
And Williamson and Gar-ner.
EDMUND FISHER.
THOSE MELODRAMA “PAPERS.”
“ Let me hut once secure the papers.’’
No character upon the stage;
Of all the good or bad ’uns,
So moves the meekest soul to rage,
So altogether maddens,
As he, or she, who travels round
A-cutting stupid capers
Until the dreary bore has found
Those melodrama “papers.'’
The-villain mostly has. the job,
And everywhere goes buzzin’
For opportunities to rob
His uncle or his cousin ;
He sports an aggravating smile
Like countermen at drapers’,
And seeks, with perseverance vdie,
Those melodrama “ papers.”
All times the gallant hero is
Absorbed in paper-chases,
And drivels through the hoary biz
In dark and lonely places ;
He rummages at dead of night
With ghostly tallow-tapers—
At last, at last he brings to light
Those melodrama “ papers !”
Or heroines, all golden hair,
On tearful travels go forth
And cry aloud, in wild despair,
For stolen wills, and so forth,
Which, as it happens, always are
Of destinies the shapers—
They make the fortune of the “ star,”
Do melodrama “ papers.”
When villains have them safe in hand
To no account they turn ’em—
So far as one can understand
They neither use nor burn ’em ;
Of idiots, in this respect,
They seem to be the apers ;
No grain of sense can one detect
In melodrama “papers.”
Of uncle’s, or the cousin’s gore
They often cause the spilling,
Though copies might be purchased for
A modest little shilling;
There’s nothing in the hollow plot
Beloved by grinning gapers
More hollow than the awful rot
Of melodrama “papers.”
Melbourne.
E.F.

Writes the San Francisco Wasp :
Does anyone remember Haxton, one of the newspaper
men who rescued the Italian fisherman out at ’Frisco
Heads ? Haxton had a remarkable personality, and men
used to turn twice to look at him on the street. He is in
London these days and has struck a streak of pay-rook ;
his articles sell everywhere, and when the brief Kipling
fever is past, Henry Haxton is as likely as anyone to be
the next writer of whom the world talks.
Henry Haxton is the husband of a gifted wife,
Agnes Thomas, an actress well-known to Aus-
tralia. When he was here he had poor health and
the world did not go well with him. He had an
inconsiderable billet ’on a weekly paper, and
The Bulletin heard nothing of him until he came
one day to this office with the MS. of “John
Yissian’s Confession,” a psychological story pub-
lished in The Bulletin Christmas number of, we
think, three years ago, and is manifestly the work
of a writer little, if at all, removed from greatness.
Mind you get Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Rev. J. F. Ewing’s sermons are out in
Melbourne, and show he died just in time to
escape being questioned for his Doxy, like
Strong and Menzies. Drummond, Ewing’s best
friend, cleared promptly out of Melbourne, under
the glowering glances of the Unco, because he
thought that a man in moleskins tied with string
round the knee-cap, could be Guid.
Insist upon having Wolfe’s Schnapps.
A Melbourne weekly broke out violently
into quotation in the middle of a sentimental tale
the other day, and this is how the naughty comp,
set it up
The doubly-wedded man and wife,
Pledged to each other and against the world
With mutual onion.
“ Mutual Onion ” is splendid. Perhaps she began
on them raw, and he joined in from motives of
self-defence.
The popular liquor—Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Thus the Argus ;—
It has been made obligatory upon the mine-owner to
count the time of the eight hours’ shifts from the dis-
appearance of the miner from the surface until he re-
appears. In some mines as much as half an hour is thus
lost in each shift, and as the men are paid for eight hours
the profits are reduced by so much.
Yes, and for the time spent by the miner in
walking to and from the job, which, in some
instances, amounts to an hour or more, his time
(which is money) is reduced by so much also.
Wolfe’s Schnapps will make you a new man.
A number of London artists’ wives recently
met to consider the advisability of issuing a pro-
test against the use of models by their husbands.
The more modest evidently thought that their
artistic hubbies should drape or undrape no other
goddess before them; but others, understanding
that an artist painting from the nude can seldom
find a model perfect enough to sit for the entire
figure, proposed a system of exchange, whereby
Flakewhite could barter away his wife’s hips for
Ohromeyellow’s spouse’s bust —and the particular
charms of any artist’s wife might become the
common artistic property of the entire guild. The
meeting ultimately broke up in great disorder.
Ask for Wolfe’s Schnapps and take no other.
Director Ferguson, of the Premier Per-
manent Society, has received an address and a
purse of sovereigns because it has been resolved
that he did not wilfully ruin a few hundred poor
people who paid him to look after their money,
but merely helped to ruin them through negligence
or ignorance, or stupidity, or all three put
together. In other words, he took a decent income
in consideration of doing a certain thing well, and
he either did it badly or left it altogether undone,
and he has kept his own money while the people
who hired him are ruined. For these reasons Mr.
Ferguson, as already mentioned, has received a
purse of sovereigns, and he took the donation
with the serene expression of a man who felt he
deserved it. The donation for the people who
have gone broke through Ferguson and his col-
leagues is not yet forthcoming.

Our Trial Crushing.
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
Tin-can Gully was in very doubtful odour
amongst investors, for its reefs had hitherto
served only as a medium for extracting calls from
the general public of Peterstown —30 miles from
us—on the coast, and whence we drew our sup-
plies. On the other hand, a good deal of alluvial
had been got in the creeks, and while the Peters-
town folk were as a rule heartily sick of
“backing ” the claims, a few people persisted in
declaring that “ the alluvial had come from some-
where,” and that a big thing would be discovered
in Tin-can Gully some day.”
It would certainly be a good thing for Peters-
town if a decent reef or two were opened up, and
we Tin-can residents knew that if we could
fossick-out something a little better than “ pay-
able ” we could get it off at a good figure.
Peterstown, much aggrieved that some of the loan-
money had not found its way to that vicinity,
badly wanted to get a railway built inland, and it
was pretty well known that old Shand, the
member, would raise Cain on the Government
benches to get one, if he had anything to show in
support.
It was just at this time that our party—there
were four of us—got on to a very likely thing,
about two feet wide, of nice-looking, rust-
stained quartz, a good deal honeycombed.
At the surface an excellent prospect of
coarse gold could be obtained in the dish,
and although it did not improve as we sank upon
it, there was a kind of unexpressed feeling
amongst us that we were “ going to make a rise.”
We made no bones about telling qur acquaint-
ances that we thought “ we were in it this time,”
and so gradually Tin-can Gully came to look upon
the “Orphan Boy ” as a claim of many possibili-
ties.
I have said that there were four in our party—
to be more accurate I should have said that there
were three working miners and one outsider, the
local publican-and-storekeeper. The working-
three consisted of myself, Jack Thelwell, who had
been my mate for seven years, and an old “Cousin
Jack ” called “ Trev.” for short, whose full name
was Arthur Trevelyan. Trev. had been working
at the alluvial for some years, but joined us when
we determined to try the “ Orphan Boy ”—-he pre-
ferred reefing, he said.
When we had been a month at work, and the
reef began to look as if it were “ going down,” old
Jansen, the publican, went into Peterstown and
managed to get four of the leading lights in that
important town to come into the thing—they
paying us a small sum for their interest, and
agreeing to bear their share of the cost of con-
tinuing the shaft. This, of course, reduced our
interest in the claim, but enabled us to keep
on without running into debt while yet we were
making no returns.
Of course we meant to float the property —when
a miner makes a rise he wants to square up his
interests and knock off work, if he can, for a year
or two at least. Thelwell and myself were deter-
mined to take a trip to England and see London
Town, if the “ Orphan Boy ” would let us.
The nearest battery was about 10 miles off, and
when we had got the shaft down 70ft. it was
determined to break a sample lot of about 10 tons
of reef-stuff, send it by team to the mill, and offer
the mine to the public on the result. We arranged
it in this way. The interests in the mine were
divided in 70,000 shares, of which each of ns had
8000—the odd 6000 were put in the hands of a
Peters town broker, who agreed to pay all expenses
in connection with the registration and flotation.
As things turned out, this was a big lift to him
for so little work, seeing that the shares were all
subscribed, so to speak—but he was known to be
a good man at “booming” a mine, and perhaps
we lost nothing by being generous.
Well, we got out the sample lot of stone, but as
the teams could not come within two or three
hundred yards of the shaft we had to bag the
stuff and carry it down to them. After the second
day’s work was done, Thelwell remarked to me, in
the tent, while we were having our supper (Old
Trev. camped at the pub): “ Jim, do you know, I
think old Trev. and Jansen are up to some games
with this crushing.”
“ How’s that?” I asked.
“Well, you know Jansen has some nice quartz
specimens which he brought with him from the
North, and which he told us one night were
worth £35.”
“Yes,” I replied. ,
“ Well, look here. This morning he and irev,
came across the flat, and when they were passing
the clay waterhole, Jansen stooped down and
seemed to be dipping something like pebbles
into the mud, which he then replaced in his
pocket. If you remember, he and Trev. bagged
up the stuff'in the forenoon, while we carried it
down to the teams. I don’t like to fancy that our
claim is being salted, but if you put two and two
together you’ll perhaps agree that you and 1
should deduct about nine ounces from the result
of the crushing.” , .
This was rather an annoying piece of news.
Salting was bad enough, but, hangdt ally salting
by our own mates ! without our being told of it..
The only thing we could do was to watch the
course of events.
The first of the teams was back, and as the
Peterstown syndicate was now up, the mill was
set a-going. Three of the Peterstown folks went
right out and stayed at the mill while the stuff
was going through, and one of them hung on
about the claim, Thelwell and myself leaving old
Trev. to help load up the last team, which was
hourly expected. Just then a very funny thing
happened. The Peterstown man and myself went
off first, and it happened that I found when we
had gone hardly half-a-mile that I had left my pipe
behind. Our camp was built on a high bluff over the
creek, and at this moment we had just come round
the bridle-track, which was very circuitous, to
the foot of a rough stair which we had made to
carry up water for our use in cooking. Slipping
off my horse, I ran up to the tent and coming
out, I happened to cast my eye over towards the
bags piled up awaiting the team—and 111 be
hanged if old Trev., whose head was turned in
another direction, was not ripping open the
sewing of the bags, while a small wash-leather
bag, apparently containing about six or eight
ounces of alluvial, was lying on the topmost bag
close to his knee ! It was quite evident that he
was going one better"than old Jansen by putting
into each bag a little washdirt of good quality !
I rejoined the rest quietly and put Thelwell up
to the dodge. “Well,” he remarked, “Isuppose
we ought to expose the thing; but, Jiin, iu
hanged if I don’t think these Peterstown fellows
are un to some racket themselves. You know that
there has been a good ten tons of stuff sent away—
nearer twelve, I believe —and this fellow with us
says that we miners are never to be relied upon
for judging weights; but that Mr. Simpson, his
chum from Peterstown, is personally seeing the
stone weighed at the mill. ” . ,
On reaching the mill that afternoon we found
that everything went well. About five tons had
gone through, there were about two tons lying on
the floor, and this, with two tons which we had
left bagged, and which the team would bring in
that evening, would make nine tons —weighed
under Mr. Simpson’s personal supervision.
But one of the hands at the mill winked at me
that evening and remarked, “Your nine tons
are taking a d long time to go through this
mill, Jim”—and, dropping his voice, he added :
“ One of the Peterstown fellows relieved me from
feeding this afternoon, while the others took me
to their buggy and gave me a wet, and they aren t
usually so blessed liberal.”
And then I figured it out mentally thus :
oz. dwt.
11 tons of stone at |oz. to the ton .. 5 10
Say that Jansen and Trev. put in specimens
equal to ®
And that Trev. added alluvial equal to .. 6 0
And that one of the Peterstown men
chucked into the stamper-boxes a few
lumps of amalgam, worth say .. .. 12 0
The clean-up, therefore, gives .. .. 33 10
And as Simpson weighed the stone as 9 tons —result,
per ton 3oz. 14dwts. lOgrs. If Jansen deducts his lOoz.
he has still a good thing in 2oz. lOdwts per ton, and if
the Peterstown folk deduct 12oz. they have still a good
thing in over 2oz. per ton, and if old Trev. deducts 16oz,
he has a good thing in nearly 2oz. per ton.

Extract from the Peterstown Star .
We are glad to announce, on excellent authority, that
the favourable opinion so long'and firmly held by many
of our oldest citizens, of the character of the Tin-can reef-
ing district, has been thoroughly and triumphantly justi-
fied by the trial crushing of about 10 tons from the
bottom of the “ Orphan boy,” p.c.—which has resulted
in a yield of nearly four ounces to the ton. The reef is
now about two feet wide, and has all the appearance of a
true fissure vein, and we are informed by several well-
known townsmen, who are largely interested, that a
battery will at once be placed on the claim.
We understand that two of the lucky proprietors have
sold their interest to their co-shareholders for a large sum,
and intend to take a well-earned voyage to the old
country. Mr. Jansen, of the “ Pick and Dish,” at Tin-
can, and Mr. Arthur Trevelyan, however, are so well
satisfied with the result, that they intend to retain their
interests in full.
We take this opportunity of again urging our worthy
member, Mr. Shand, to bring the rich mineral resources
of the Tin-can district prominently before the Ministry,
with a view to opening up railway communications, &c.

George Bates, who settled on Kangaroo
Island and lived with the natives long before the
South Australian mainland was proclaimed and
occupied, is still alive. He is between 90 and 100
years old, but very feeble. The old man has
hyed on the Island, with no companion but his
wife, who is also alive, for nearly 60 years.
Mrs. O’Shea’s sister, Mrs. Steel, was in
the British divorce-court some years ago, “not over
a breach of her marriage vows, but merely for
their non-performance. As a romantic and love-
stricken girl she accepted Captain Steel on the
understanding that they should live apart. And
acting on this understanding she parted from her
husband at the church door, and never rejoined
him in his home. Years afterwards, finding that
his strange misfortune was a cloud on his profes-
sional career, he commenced a suit for the restitu-
tion of conjugal rights by way of clearing his
character, but never acted on the decree which he
triumphantly obtained. In fact, there was no
defence, and no other explanation beyond that
general family eccentricity which has led Mrs.
O’Shea to sacrifice her life for an Irish squireen,
without humour or small talk, and wearing muddy
boots, an ill-fitting frock-coat, and a soft brown
felt hat! ”
n , _ , ,
lhat gathering of solemn fiats known as
the Presbyterian Assembly rises reproachfully to
grab for the coppers as usual, and to prop up the
falling heavens with its old bald head. Listen :
The fruit and lolly shops are kept open for certain hours
of the Lord’s day under sanction of the law, and they are
traps to catch the children’s pennies designed for the
mission-box or the Sunday School. Every effort has been
used to lessen this Shocking Sin, and with some measure
of success.
It is the same old church as it always was, and the
same old parsons babbling about motes and atoms,
and clamouring after the baby’s half-penny in the
same grand old way. The dull, flabby, boneless
sin-shifter is always haunted by the fear that sax-
pence has gone bang in needless worldly in-
diligence instead of being given to him, as it ought
to be. He doesn’t bother much about war, or
disease, or any large questions of that description;
but the Shocking Bin which haunts him, like a
blue-moulded spectre rising on the wings of night,
is the fear that the coming race may invest its
copper in a clammy bun or a stick of toffee, so
that when he turns the miserable infant upside
down and shakes it in the name of the Loard
there will be no worldly assets left to fall out of
its pocket. Then he realises that the insolvent
state of that interted child of wrath is em-
phatically a Wrong Thing, and the pain and
indignation that are written upon his long, pale,
dreary countenance have to find vent in print, or
he will burst under the shadow of his own black
hat from the pressure of his inward emotion He
is only an ant cursing at the sun, but then he
doesn’t know it, and if anyone explains the fact to
him he mentions regretfully that his informant is
going to hell.

every bird of prey will sell his lease to a company
at a high figure, and when the company bursts he

without endorsement, and he will liquidate it,

came about. The public always wonders a good

One of the most striking features of Mel-
bourne is its assortment of cripples. They are a
multitudinous race decorated with every known
variety of deformity and distortion, but whether
Melbourne grows its own cripples, or whether it
imports them, or whether they are artificially
manufactured, and form an industry instead of a
natural product, is uncertain. The average boot-
maker by Yarra’s crystal tide always exhibits in
his shop-window at least a dozen samples of boots
to fit as many different varieties of cripples,
There are boots for the man with two left legs
and no right one, and boots for the man with two
right legs and no left one, and hoof-pear for the
person with the club-foot—an individual who is
very numerous. Also the trade in artificial legs is
tremendous, and it is never safe to hit a passing
stranger in the street, for as likely as not he bad
his arm shot off when he was young and wears an
iron hook instead, and when he turns and coldly
sticks that implement into your head you are
liable to die of shock and amazement. Then
there are a strange variety of persons drifting
about the city who have had all their hair and
features blown off by an accident in a mine, and
persons who seem to have been frittered away in
an effort to pound themselves to death against a
brick wall; and parties who travel with a crutch,
or two crutches, or a crutch and a stick, or a pail'
of sticks, and several anomalous citizens pervade
Bourke-street on their hands because they never
had any at all. One of this latter class
garroted an inoffensive visitor recently, and when
a policeman endeavoured to arres£ him he fought
three rounds with the officer, and rurally fled in a
locomotive chair, and has not since been heard of.
Also there is a large and varied assortment of
hump-backed inhabitants, and a profusion of
knock-kneed men whose extremities spread to
such an extent that they • generally have one foot
on the staircase and the other protruding through
the window, and why all these people exist is a
mystery that no one has ever attempted to solve.
The old and hoary dodge of our fathers
has been repeated with the usual disastrous
results at Wellington (N.Z.), and another respect-
able family has gone into mourning over the
corpse of its dead reputation. The principal
sinner Was an old and esteemed Government
official, and the other person involved in the
transaction was his wife. It Was a peaceful,
moonlight night, with the ghost of a lost zephyr
rustling faintly among the lulls, and the male
malefactor, for purposes of his own, had stated
that he would be detained till a late hour toiling
in the service of an ungrateful nation. She said
that under these circumstances she would spend
the night with her sister. But about two hours
later he was floating serenely down the harbour
with two wildly dissipated friends and three
second-rate actresses, and they were howling a
comic song in chorus, and the ladies had borrowed
their belltoppers and had lent their own bonnets
in exchange, so that the party looked like six
escaped Aunt Sallies all liquoring up on the
bosom of the sea; and just then they nearly ran
down another boat loaded with a crowd of
revellers who were also whooping a song, and
throwing empty champagne - bottles overboard.
And from that other boat, as the two vessels
grazed each other, there came the shriek of a
deceived wife out on the rampage with two other
ladies and six vociferous male companions—
a shriek that seemed to be compounded of a
railway smash and a drunken boarder trying to get
upstairs on his hands, and an 801 b. dumb-bell
falling into a dinner-service, and a lion breaking-
up a tea-meeting, and a rhinoceros charging a
stock of galvanised iron ; and the guilty pair
realised that their scheme was exploded. Both
were seized at once with the idea of establishing
an alibi, and the two boats fled in two different
directions for the shore, and when they got on land
each party started at a run for home with the idea
of getting into bed and being fast asleep before
the other arrived, but this project unhappily fell
through. The lady nearly killed a tired cab-
horse on her journey, but just as she reached the
door her husband rounded the opposite corner on
foot with his hat gone and all the buttons started
off his waistcoat, and they met. Then they
repudiated the marriage-tie upon the doorstep,
and finally the wife departed in hysterics in the
bottom of the cab, and took the key with her, and
the locked-out husband slept at a pub. It is the
same old trouble that the human race has kept
falling into ever since Joseph was made a co-
respondent and Potipher went looking for a decree
nisi in Egypt, and it had the same old ending. It
always does, somehow.
u One tale is good until another is told.”
There is evidently no love lost between Francis
Hare, P.M., and Power, the bushranger, if we
are to draw an inference from the rival accounts
of the latter’s capture. In the LkadEh of Nov, 15
Superintendent Hare takes all the glory upon
himself, and says that Power was nothing but a
coward, and that he effected the arrest almost as
easily as he would that of an ordinary drunk and
disorderly. He also intimates that Power could
draw the long bow as well as, if not better, than
most people. In the Standard of Dec. 16 Power
retorts that the P.M. does not tell the truth, and
that it was he himself who was the coward—that,
in revenge for having his head dipped in a bag of
flour, he endeavoured to shoot him (Power) when
Nicholson had him handcuffed, and that Messrs,
Nicholson and Montford were in reality the
captors. Someone will now proceed to pass the
salt.
Mr. Herbert Reynolds, a very shining
light amongst English Dissenters, writes in a
melancholy strain to the effect that the present
Sunday-school system has been a failure, whereas
every close observer of such matters knows that it
has proved an unqualified success up to now.
Without Sunday-schools life would be a dreary
waste for hundreds of meek-eyed young females,
and weak-kneed young men, who find a bond of
sympathy in the interesting legend of Jonah and
the Whale. Ruth and Obadiah look on Bible*
classes as stepping-stones to the courtship which
begins in long walks home together, and
occasionally culminates in a spicy “maintenance ”
case. Being both of a nervous, blushful, retiring
disposition they would die of suppressed love bub
for the blessed opportunities of touching one
another’s tingling fingers when they borrow Bibles
or exchange bundles of tracts, or both push up the
school-rpom window at the same time, at which
ecstatic moments they always maintain a sickly
appearance of innocence and breathe hard. For
these young people. Sunday-schools, with the
picnics appertaining thereto, serve as outlets for
sanctified giddiness, just as balls and carpet-
dances fill a long-felt want in more earthly circles.
Ruth’s secret yearning for the masculine hug is
every bit as strong as that of her frivolous cousin
Ethel, but whilst the latter likes a gentle squeezing
to the accompaniment of dance-music, the former
prefers that Obadiah shall hum a hymn-tune
during the ceremony. So Ruth takes a Sunday-
school class, and in due course Obadiah comes
along, in a hesitating sort of way, with his first
kiss of Christian love. Even if the osculation be
only of a brotherly character it gets there all the
same. Apart from the opportunities they offer for
full-grown mashing, Sunday-schools are of inestim-
able benefit to the children. The missionary-box
is a grand institution for enabling little Tommy
to substitute hardbake for heathen when dis-
bursing his Sabbath penny. “Be sure you put it
in the box, Tommy,” says his confiding mother.
He does put the penny in the slot and gets a cake
of toffee in return. Sometimes he gets a cigarette.
Under cover of attending Sunday-school, the
little innocents of both sexes acquire general
knowledge at an appalling rate, and as for real fun
—why the pantomime, the bad, unregenerate pan-
tomime, is a fool to the orgies that take place in
the Church grounds. Sunday-schools a failure I
Reynolds must be getting at us.

Robert Buchanan on Parnell’s Adultery.
Robert Buchanan thus addresses London
Echo on the Parnell-O’Shea case in a letter-
headed “ The Journalist in Absolution ” :—
Writing neither as a person having authority,
nor as one of the scribes, I wish to put on record,
if you will permit me, my complete and absolute
sympathy with Mr. Parnell. He may, or may
not, be an adulterer, —that in any case, I con-
sider a detail, chiefly interesting to himself ; but I
contend that his technical and legal guilt is no
proof whatever of his moral turpitude. No ques-
tion involving the relation of the sexes can be ab-
solutely decided in the tainted atmosphere of our
foul Divorce Court, and the case of “O’Shea v.
Parnell ” was established by the unworthiest of all
evidence, that of prying chambermaids, prurient
lodging-house keepers, and all the miserable
human fry who swim in the unclean shallows of
the legal puddle. To my mind, Mr. Parnell’s
stern and absolute silence, his determination not
to be dragged through the obscene mire, is nega-
tive evidence in his favour. He has chosen, likea
strong man, to let the blow fall on his own
shoulders, and the result is that Mrs. O’Shea has
been spared and almost forgotten, while all the
moral wolves are clamouring for Mr. Parnell’s
blood. But even if Mr. Parnell is guilty no man
can tell in what degree. That, as 1 have said, is
a matter chiefly concerning himself. What con-
cerns us, men who stand as simple spectators of a
persecution unparalleled in the history of politics,
is the means which are being adopted to hound a
great man out of public life.
It is on record, I believe—or at any rate it has
been stated —that immediately after the decision
of the Divorce Court a well-known journalist
waited upon Mr. Parnell and informed him that
unless.full “confession” was made at once and
the leadership of the Irish party simultaneously
resigned, the said journalist would appeal to the
Puritans of England to “let loose the dogs” of
moral War. Whether threatened or not, the
thing has been done, and Mr. Parnell has been
hunted down, not by honest public opinion, not
by British virtue, not even by the British Matron,
but by the Journalism of the sewers on the one
side and the Journalism of the back-kitchen on
the other. For whence chiefly arises this
ferocious clamour of prurient morality, this talk
about the sanctity of the household, and the
eternal symbolism of the bed-post ? Firstly, from
the source out of which arose the publication of a
scandal so infamous, and described so imfamously,
that the very air of Nature was polluted as by a
cesspool, the stench of which penetrated like
poison into every household of the land.
Secondly, from the individual who invented the
journalism of Paul Pry, who has violated
all the privileges of social life, while haunt-
ing the back-kitchens of the aristocracy,
and counting the candle-ends of the govern-
ing classes; and who, finally, proposed not
long ago, in the Commons, to the manifest satis-
faction of a crowd of fellow-demagogues, to pol-
lute the ears of his fellow-members by opening up
in broad day the sewer of another foul and loath-
some scandal. The other attacks on the character
of the member for Cork may be set aside as purely
political. The attacks to which I draw attention
are specifically “ moral.” It is the latter to which
I wish to confine your attention, while demanding
whether we are to substitute for the espionage of
the old and discredited priesthoods the espionage
of the Journalist in Absolution ?
No “Confessional Unmasked ” has yet, to my
mind, furnished so sad an illustration of human
prurience as the new Confessional of the Journal.
Manifold as are the injuries which Journalism in
general has done to society, to literature, and to
art, by fostering the uninstruction of the general
reader, and parodying the ephemeral judgments
of'the hour, those injuries are small to the crimes
committed by the Journalism which masquerades
in the guise of Morality, which deals in household
garbage, and, in the interests of vulgar curiosity,
institutes a Public Confessional. Dismal, indeed,
is the lot of the human being who, like Mr. Par-
nell, sits in the confession-box, with the Priest of
Prurience on one side and the Priest of Espionage
on the other. If he refuses, as Mr. Parnell has
done, to make any kind of utterance, woe to him
and to his generation ! The flood-gates of denun-
ciation are opened ; the whole army of back-
kitchen moralists and scandal-mongers is arrayed
against him ; the standard of the Cross is raised,
and men prepare for the luxury of the auto da ft.
Honest citizens bar their doors, and peep from
their window's in terror. Everywhere, ushered by
the newsboy with his “latest edition,” w T alk the
agents of the Inquisition.
To most men who would live their lives in peace
Journalism is merely Babbage’s Organ in the
Street: they stop their ears, and try to think and
work in spite of it. But to all men who value the
security of their homes and the right of private
judgment the New Journalism, with its aggression,
its tyrannical bias, and its shameless indecency,
is the old Priest in Absolution forcing a way into
every household. Tartuffe and Melchior live
again in the columns of the inquisitorial news-
paper, while the Soapin of Politics walks hand in
hand with the Mawworm of Morality. At this
moment, therefore, when a wave of prurient Puri-
tanism is rising higher and higher to destroy all
that makes the world sweet and wholesome, it is
with no common interest that we who are neither
inquisitorial nor “ moral ” watch the fate of Mr.
Parnell. If he stands like a rock, refusing to be
doomed by the Divorce Court, and defying the
clamour of penny-a-Hning Pharisees, there is still
hope for Society. If he falls, bestrade! led over
by the rampant Journalist in Absolution, wo
who loathe his would-be Confessors may well
despair. I shall say nothing here of his
public services, of his power and prescience as
the one man capable of interpreting the hopes
and wishes of the Irish race; nothing of the
constitutional bigotry which has led even so honest
a man as Mr. Gladstone to join in the cry against
him. It should be remembered, nevertheless,
that Mr. Parnell retains his position not because
he is privately virtuous, but because he is politi-
cally puissant, and that Mr. Gladstone, despite
all his noble disinterestedness, is a retrograde
moralist, who repudiates Divorce under any cir-
cumstances, and founds his repudiation on the
diseased ravings of mediaeval monks and saints.
I for one believe that issues far deeper than any
issues merely political will be determined by the
ultimate position of Mr. Parnell. I for one refuse
to accept the discredited disclosures of the Divorce
Court and the obscene comments of the Journalist
in Absolution as any final test of human life and
character. —I am, &c.,
Robert Buchanan.

The Mount Morgan mine last year yielded
£475,958 worth of gold. In one memorable year
it paid away £1,200,000 in dividends.
Civil Service slaves may be interested to
know that there are more public holidays in
Honolulu than in any other city in the world.
Among the days observed are Queen’s Birthday,
Coronation Day, all the French and Portuguese
holidays, the American Thanksgiving Day, and
the Chinese New Year, as well as all the public
holidays peculiar to the gay and festive Honolulu
cusses themselves.
The gentle, forgiving soul of woman
asserted itself lately in the Auckland police-court.
A stout, middle-aged lady was called by the police
as a witness in a larceny-case. She came up
rather reluctantly, and kissed the Scripture- with
the air of a person tackling an uncongenial job,
and then a bright idea seemed to cross her intel-
lect for she smiled a great, happy smile, and fired
the Bible straight at the nearest lawyer's head.
He had just time tobobdown as the missile grazed
his ear, and broke its back against the opposite
wall, and then a brawny officer rushed forward
and seized the bearer of evidence, and the word
of Truth was picked up and dusted, and the court
adjourned.

Melbourne Age still keeps harping on the
iniquity of constructing non-profitable railway
lines. As it is not a question of profit, but rather
one of convenience, we are beginning to feel tired.
Of course it is not the correct thing to study tiro
'■convenience of one person or batch of individuals,
but where the interests of a large body of indivi-
duals are concerned, provided that the State does
not unduly favour one section of the community
at the expense of another, it is morally bound to
afford all its members similar means of transit
regardless of primary cost.
'1 he yearly increment on the London
rental (cap tal value) is over four-and-a-half
millions. This increase, of course, in site-value
only, and in no way dependent on individual im-
provement of buildings, is a communally-produced
value,.unearned by the landlords who pocket it.
The houses of London are worth, it is supposed,
£212,000,000 ; the land .on which they stand is
estimated at £418,000,000. On the latter sum the
landlords receive about £17,100,000 annually as
rent, and year by year, under the leasehold sys-
tem, the houses fall into their hands.
One of the neatest bits of shuffling lately
performed by Premier Parkes was that reply to
Premier Munro on the Booth “ In Darkest Eng-
land ” question. Asked to state his intentions
with regard to the reception his Government
would afford to the threatened swarm of British
refuse, he returned that having carefully read the
book he felt convinced that the colony referred to
by the General mpst be Africa, not Australia,
and hence there was no need to discuss the matter
at present. This is the return snub for that little
reply of Dunky’s on the “ Australia ” vice “New
South Wales ” circular.

members of the Cabinet strongly disapprove of
the action of the late Government in undertaking

three personal friends of the Cabinet wei’e prose-
cuted (one of them having been convicted), and

accounting for the presence of so much milk of
human kindness in the Government cocoa-nut.
Wolfe’s Schnapps—look for £1 orders.
The papers which are creating such a dust
because Parnell declines to give way to Gladstone
would do well to remember that Parnell never
sought the alliance with Gladstone —it was Glad-
stone who sought Parnell, and presumably he did
it for his own purposes. Consequently, if Glad-
stone goes the party will only have lost an acci-
dental ally ; if Parnell goes it will have lost the
man who created it, and made it what it is. The
man who borrowed Home Rule seeks to bounce
the man who made it, and is wildly surprised to
find that the latter refuses to regard himself as a
mere secondary party in the transaction.
Wolfe’s Schnapps will do you no harm.
Ex-Gov. darington’s “For Ever,” in his
prediction about the esto perpelua of Australian
subjection to England, is like Munro’s never-
forgotten “Never, never, never!” when he de-
clared the stone wall would never give way before
the iron hand —yet it did. But Carington
only speaks like Carlyle’s Chancellor in the
“French Revolution.” He used to gyrate and
gyrate, and say “ it will last my time.” That is
the meaning of for ever—“ after me the Deluge.”
What cares Carington about the time when his
barouche no longer conveys him to the Marl-
borough ? What has posterity done for us - the
peerage ? Why, it’s our destruction.

Under the circumstances of the Behring
Strait difficulty, it is interesting to know
that last year the United States Govern-
ment resolved that, instead of a comparatively
insignificant fleet, there should be an array of new
ships costing £55,000,000 sterling—the entire pro-
gramme comprising 227 vessels, including 10 battle-
ships of 10,000 tons; 28 second-class ironclads,
G000 to 8000 tons ; 7 monitors, 3600 to 6000 tons ;
10 rams, 3500 tons; 35 very fast cruisers, 3500 to
7000 tons ; 6 cruisers, 1700 to 3200 tons ; 10 gun-
boats, 16 torpedo-catchers, 101 torpedo-boats.
Secretary Tracy writes, in his Annual Report for
this year : “The defence of the U.S. absolutely
requires the creation of a fighting force.”

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill 1 consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytiie, J.P.
